and the Socialists 244.

THERE was no contest to speak
of in Democratic circles yester
day. A majority of the Demo
crats participating In the pri
mary election cast their votes for
Republican delegates. It la
known that 300 Democrats belonging to
the Ralney-Maxwell forces of the party
joined hands with Martin Kelly yesterday
to defeat the Republican Primary League
ticket In the Forty-fourth Assembly Dis
trict. They signed the voting roll as Re
publicans and did not hesitate when chal
lenged to swear that they intended to vote
the Republican ticket at the municipal
election. It is estimated that fully 5000
Democrats went to the polls to help the
Republican bosses. Coin was freely cir
culated. In the entire city only 3633 Dem
ocratic ballots were called for and voted
yesterday. The fact is clearly demon
strated that the primary election law
must be amended.
Mayor's GityHall Push
Dutifully Votes
His Tickets*
Majority of Democrats
Cast Kelly-Crim-
nuns blips*
KELLYITES
RESORT TO
TRICKERY
MARTIN KELLY, CHIEF OF THE COMBINED
BOSSES, IS WINNER AT PRIMARY ELECTION
HOW AID
IS GIVEN TO
BIG BOSS
Democrats Help Him
Out in Various
Districts*
'R&mey and Kelly Arc
Prepared to Sell
Nominations*
THE allied bosses, under the lead
ership of Martin Kelly, were- suc
cessful at the election yesterday.
In tie Fortieth District the Re
publican Primary League ticket.
headed by Thomas P. Woodward,
president of the league, was elected. In
several other districts tickets Indorsed by
the league were successful. The bosses
cla^m that Kelly has sufficient strength
to organize the Republican municipal con
vention and dictate the nominations, and
the claim appears to bo well founded.
For reasons best known to themselves
E2#X) registered voters refused or neglect
ed to participate in the primary election.
It Is easy enough to trace some of the
reasons for this apparent Indifference. No
doubt many of the 20.003 Republicans who
did not go near the polling places were
simply disgusted or disheartened by the
shameless conduct of the Republican
State Central Committee In lending Its
indorsement to the Kelly-Crimmlns Coun
ty Committee. Others refrained from vot
ing to preserve the right of petition to
place on the official ballot candidates
other than those deriving nomination
from the regular party convention. The
absence from the polls of the great mass
of Republican voters ran be ascribed to
general indifference to the management of
municipal affairs. The regular politicians,
the place hunters and spoilsmen partici
rated actively. Money was freely and
openly used to buy votes for the boss
ticket.
Democrats Sick of Phelan.
Twenty thousand Democrats who are
wesry and sick of the Phelan administra
tion refrained from voting. No doubt 5000
Democrats were rallied to vote for Re
publican delegates and nearly all of them
voted the Kelly-Crimmlns boss ticket.
The strength of the anti-boss Republican
column was overcome by the Ralney
Democratic push. In precincts where
Democratic majorities axe heaviest the
vote for Republican delegates was largest.
Democrats readily signed the . roll as
Republicans and were readily provided
with Republican ballots. It Is a weakness
of the primary election law that the push
politicians of toth parties may combine
to control the nominations of one or the
other of the conventions. Yesterday only
400G votes were thrown for delegates to
the Democratic municipal convention.
These votes were cast mainly by the reg
tilars of the Phelan-McXab-McDonald or
ganization.
The issue involved In the" election was
clearly presented to the people of San
Francisco by The CalL By declining to
participate In the election the voters of
â¢the Republican party signified their will
ingness to accept boss domination in the
convention. This journal made a clean,
consistent fight for the principles of good
local government. It had no candidates
to advocate. It had no schemes to pro
mote. It was grided by a purpose to pro
mote the good of the city, and It still ad
heres to that pnroose.
Independent Delegates May Bevolt.
Perhaps the independent citizens whose
names were placed on Boss Kelly's tickets
Jn BeveTal of the districts where the
Ixwses -were not cure of their strength will
revolt and refuse to take orders from
Martin Kelly, but they may not be able
to do more than enter protest against the
methods of bosslsm.
The delegates placed before the people
by the Republican Primary League rep
resented the true anti-boss spirit of the
party. The allied bosses under the leader
ship of the adroit and corrupt Martin
Kelly defeated the candidates of the
league. It would be useless to underesti
mate Kelly's triumph. He planned thÂ»
campaign In advance. It was by his order
that a provision was placed in the primary
election law giving the State Central
Committee authority to Interfere and
recognize a county committee. The law
was framed to cover this contest In San
Francisco.
Kelly Has Nominations to Sell.
Boss Kelly expects to sell the Repub
lican nominations to the highest bidder.
Smaller bosses may attempt to break In
for a share of the boodle, but Kelly Is not
the kind of a fellow to weaken and give
Jn after going through the political tun
nel for the treasure. Lynch, Herrln. Mac
kenzie and others of his followers may
bluster around and threaten dire conse
quences If a proper division of the spoils
Is not guaranteed, but noise will not
avail. Boss Kelly having gone to the
front to make the flgrht Is entitled as a
Â¦winner to make the division of the plun
der. He is the chief of the bosses. He
Is a delegate to the convention. His name
heads the successful delegation In the
Twenty-eighth Assembly District. It will
be the first name called on the roll of the
convention. Republicans of San Fran
cisco may not fancy the leadership, but
they permitted the Infamy of Kelly's tri
umph to be placed on the city. Twenty
thousand registered Republican citizens

Mrs. J. W. Folger Injured.
DEL MONTE, Aug. 13.â Mrs.J.W.Folger
of San Francisco was seriously injured
while driving from here to Pacific Grove
to-day. Her horse stumbled and fell and
immediately began kicking violently. Mrs.
Folger, fearing the frightened animal
would bolt, jumped from the trap. She
fell upon one leg, breaking the ligaments
between the knee and ankle so badly that
she will be unable to walk for some time.

CHARLESTON IS DENIED Â» ,
USE OF STATE EXHIBIT
COLUMBUS, Ohio, Aug. 13.â Attorney
General Sheets has sent for a transcript
of the testimony of President Schwab of
the United States Steel Corporation be
fore the Industrial Commission at Wash
ington for use in a proposed investigation
into the concern's status under the State
anti-trust laws. He declined to state his
pians further than this.
Attorneys here think the United States
Steel Corporation can defy : the State offi
cials Â¦'for the reason .that it has simply
bought a majority of the stock of the
constituent â¢ companies ; In this State â not
the full properties. For this reason the
United States Steel Corporation is neither
a trust under the Ohio law nor a foreign
corporation doing business in this State.
The Secretary of State will to-morrow
forward to the president of the United
States Steel Corporation blanks similar to
those sent to other foreign corporations
doing business in the State, notifying the
corporations that the State is ready to
receive the tax due it under the law ap
plying to foreign corporations. If the
company refuses to pay the tax suit will
at once be brought to collect it. If the
corporation consents to pay the tax it
will have qualified, it is claimed, merely
to do a legal business in the State. The
Secretary of State and Attorney General
say they are convinced that the constitu
ent companies are doing- an illegal busi
ness in the State in that they are con
nected with a trust, and, whether the
United States Steel Corporation pays the
tax or not, proceedings will be brought
against the Ohio companies under the
anti-trust law.
The State of Ohio has no jurisdiction
over the United States Steel Corporation
as a whole, and if the corporation's re
turn to the Secretary of State satisfies
him that it cannot qualify to do business
in Ohio it is possible that the Attorney
General of the United States and the At
torneys General of all States in which the
steel trust operates may'be asked to bring
proceedings against it. Attorney General
Sheets admitted that such a course is
within the range of possibilities, but de
clined to make a statement as to his
probable action.
Investigation of Its Stat
us! to Be Made in
Ohiol ,

ROCKLIN, Aug., 13.-A Knights of Pyth
ias lodge with sixty charter members, a
large proportion of whom are employes
of the Southern Pacific Railroad Com
pany, was organized here to-night by
Grand - Chancellor Calkins, aided by
knights from Newcastle, Auburn, Lincoln
and Folscm. Robert F. Burns of Auburn
was the instituting officer. At midnight
a banquet was served. It is proposed to
organize a uniform rank company, a sec
tion of the endowment rank and a tem
ple of Rathbone Sisters here.

Frank Engle, residing at 6V2 Grove
street, was arrested yesterday at the poll
ing place on the coiner of Hyde street and
Golden Gate avenue by Officers G. O.
Brown and J. E. Ward on a charge of at
tempting to vote illegally. Engle. when
arraigned at the booking desk in the City
Prison, said that be was accosted by a
young man when passing the polling
booth and asked to vote. A slip was
handed him, on which was written .the
name of Joseph Kiernan of 100 McAllister
street. Engle entered the booth and
asked for a ballot. He was challenged,
and as he could not answer the questions
put to him he was ordered into custody.
He says he does not know the .name of
the man. -who asked him to vote.

LONDON.- Aug. 13.â At to-day's session
of the South African Compensation Com
mission Newton Crane, counsel for the
American claimants, made further repre
sentations regarding the claims of nine
men who were expelled for complicity in
the plot against Lord Roberts. Major
General Sir John C. Ardagh. representing
the Foreign Office, read documentary evi
dence from the military authorities, in
cluding a letter from Lord Kitchener, to
the effect that United States Consul Adel
bert.S. Hay had been invited to visit the
men In prison and had declined to guar
antes the Integrity of these nine or their
worthiness to be allowed to remain. When
General Ardagh read an affidavit alleging
that James G. Maloney of New York, one
cf the claimants, was an "extremely im
pecunious drunkard and an associate of
ex-convicts." Crane interposed, saying:
"I'll inquire into the truth of the state
ments, and if they are substantiated the
American Government's pressure in re
gard to this claim will be circumscribed."
Chairman Milvan jocularly replied:
"We won't go to war about this." â¢>
Crane, in setting forth the position of
the American Government at the close of
the hearing, said that with two excep
tion?. Great Britain had made no explana
tion-of the arrest or deportation of Amer
ican citizens. He denied the right of de
portation either in peace or war until evi
dence had been taken of an infraction of
the law of hostility to the Government.
The United States would not be bound by
any narrow legal rule as to the damages,
but expected that the broadest considera
tion would be given to the demands of
these of her citizens who had been im
properly arrested and deported. Jt was
no excuse to say they had been given full
soldiers' rations and traveling accommo
dations. The American claimants were
net -soldiers. They were subjected to bru
tal physical sufferings and must have
compensation.
General Ardagh suggested that one of
the claimants should give oral testimony
and said that the British Government
would pay the witness' expenses. Mr.
Crane accepted the suggestion.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 13.â The American
claims which are being presentd to the
South African Compensation Commission,
now sitting at London, are understood to
be those, presented to the State Depart
ment' and forwarded to Embassador
Choate. The Embassador was asked to
put the claims In the proper channel for
formal presentation and he was author
ized to secure the services of such coun
sel as migrht be necessary. It is probable
that Newton Crane, who Is referred to as
counsel for the American claimants, is
acting under this authorization or he may
be looking after the individual interests of
the claimants.

SAVED FBOM DROWNING
IN THE SACRAMENTO
Assemblyman Hubbard and a Friend
Companions.
Rescued by v Two Brave
REDDING, Aug. 13.â Assemblyman J.
A. Hubbard of Shasta and Modoc coun
ties, and Charles Keeler, a young man of
Anderson, in which town Mr. Hubbard
has his home, narrowly escaped death by
drowning to-day In- the Sacramento Rivi
err. They were saved by the prompt ac
tion of their comrades.
Hubbard and Keeler are members of
the Alfalfa Club, a pleasure organization
of Anderson, and In company with ex-
Senator Frank Sprague and eight other
members of the club, started to take
a ride down the Sacramento River from
Anderson to Balls Ferry in a hastily con
structed raft. In passing beneath the
Terry railroad bridge the raft struck a
pier, 5 and Hubbard and Keeler were
thrown into the water. Hubbard struck
a timber and was Injured to such an ex
tent, that he could not swim. Simon L.
Rodshund, one of the party and a strong
swimmer, went to the injured man's as
sistance. He succeeded in getting him
ashore after a hard strugrgle. Keeler
could swim but little, but George Storey,
another one from the raft, swam to him
and carried him to shore on his back. .
BOSTON, Aug. 13.-Joseph A. Conroy ia
the Democratic Congressman-elect from
the Ninth Massachusetts District and one
of the leading members of his party in
this city. Joseph K. Norton is a member
of the Boston Board of Aldermen. It is
thought here that the third member of
the party is Patrick Bowen, a Boston
Alderman, who is in New York.
Conroy received a stab wound in the
back and Francis was wounded in the
thigh. The wounds of the injured men
were dressed and they said they would
make no complaint, even if the police suc
ceeded in finding the man who did the
stabbing.
From what could be learned at the hotel
three Boston politicians, whose names the
police give as Joseph A. Conroy. Patrick
Bowen and Joseph Norton, started the
fight. The Wall Paper Manufacturers'
convention is being held at the Gilsey
House, and a number of the delegates
were standing in front of the doorway
when the three men crossed the street to
enter the hotel. Conroy, it is asserted,
had some words with some of the paper
men, and in a moment a lively fight was
in progress. William F. Francis, also
said to be a wall paper manufacturer, was
attracted to the scene from a neighbor
ing restaurant and went to the assistance
of his friends.

HONOLULU. H. T., Aug. 6.â A fire

ICEW YORK, Aug. 13.â Helen Potts
Hall, who Is suing to recover the large es
tate of the late George Francis Gilman,
obtained an absolute divorce to-day from
Blakely Hall, the proprietor of several
publications, by a decree of Justice .Mc-
Lean of the Supreme Court. By order of
the court all papers In the case except the
decree were sealed, and the decree seems
to have been drawn to avoid any expres
sion of facts not absolutely essential. The
case was heard before a referee, but his
name Is not given. Such a trial Involved
the denial of charges made by Mrs. Hall
and an agreement of counsel for both
Fides. The name of the attorney for Hall
does not appear in the decree, and no ob
jection was made on his behalf. to the
confirmation of the report of the referee.

COLORADO SPRINGS, Aug. 13.-The
fact has just come to light that General
W. J. Palmer of this city, at the time
that Jie sold his interest in the Rio Grande
Western Rairoad for $6,000,000, about three
months ago. made a distribution of 1000
shares of the stock of the company, then
at 104, among the employes of the road,
amounting in all to : more than a million
dollars. Each employe, from section fore
man to general manager, was remem
bered, Vice " President I>>dge receiving
more than $100,000 worth pt stock.
Grande "Western, Shows His
Generosity.
General Palmer, Formerly of the Hio
GIVES THE EMPLOYES
A MILLION IN STOCK
"I myself, assisted by Charles N. ..Post
and George A. Sturtevant, will conduct
the prosecution. The outrage was so
gross, henlous and glaring that the whole
power of the State will be employed to
punish them, and, as I said before, we are
certain of bringing the ringleaders to an
swer for their crime. The punishment of
the convicted men will be either death or
life imprisonment."
"The trial will not take place until some
time in September, as the defendants have
got out a writ of prohibition against Judge
J. H. Harrington of Modoc County, and
that â¢will delay the case for a couple of
months. The State has more evidence
than it needs right now and Is ready to go
on with the trial to-morrow. We know
the name of every man connected with
the lynching and the exact degTee of cul
pability of each individual, and we will
make them wish they had respected the
law befor^we get through with them.
"About a month ago I sent Eugene
Thacker, a son of the Wells-Fargo detec
tive, and Fred H. Jackson up to Modoc
County to obtain evidence- against the
lynchers. They have been working in the
vicinity of Lookout ever since and haye
got enough evidence to make certain the
conviction of Eades, Brown and Levan
ton, the three men now -in custody for
participation in the outrage. You can also
publish the fact that these three men are
not the only ones who will be made to
answer for the hanging.
When shown the foregoing dispatch last
evening. Attorney General Tirey L. Ford
said:
"Thty were not a very brave lot of men
and they are now thoroughly cowed," said
Jackson. "They are all equally guilty and
will have to pay the penalty. I paid abso
lutely no attention to their warnings and
threats, and think I can -prove their
guilt."
Jackson says that the hanging of the
five men was entirely unjustifiable. He
reels confident, that a clear case N can be
made out against each member of the
lynching party, the names of whom he
had, but refused to divulge.
Jackscn is employed as a Wells-Fargo
messenger on the Jackson and lone stage
and made himself famous in the battle
which resulted in the death of John Son
tag and the capture of Chris Evans, |in
which fight he lost a foot. Shortly after
the Mcdoc lynching he appeared in this
vicinity, ostensibly to spend several
months at his wife's former home on
Churn Creek Bottom, near Redding, but
In reality to assist the State in establish
ing the identity of the lynchers. The pub
lic in general failed to suspect the occa
sion of his visit, but the guilty members
of the midnight party of Lookout did so
immediately. It now seems that their
representatives stopped at a Churn Creek
Bottom home, where Jackson spent a few
days as a blind, and, waiting upon him,
warned him to depart. Unmindful of this.
Jackson went straight to the stronghold
of the lynchers and, avowing the purpose
of his visit, began his â¢investigation. He
was again waited upon after his arrival
there, but declined to take cognizance of
threats made against him. He completed
his investigation . and departed without
molestation.
REDDING, Aug. 13.â Every member of
the mob which took Calvin Hall and his
three sons and Daniel Yantis from the
custody of a Constable and his deputies
at Lookout, Modoc County, several
months t ago and hanged them to a bridge
near town will be brought to justice. So
declared Fred H. Jackson, who has made
an investigation of the late tragedy for
Attorney General Tirey L. Ford, and who
passed through here last night on the way
to San Francisco to make his report.
The steel corporation held its ground at
Chicago, Joliet, Bayview, Youngstown and
Columbus, the Kiskiminetas Valley and
the Carnegie group, with the exception of
the instance named. The strikers are con
sidering plans for breaking the decision
of Chicago, Joliet and Bayview to stand
â¢with the steel companies, but what they
plan to do has not been made clear. They
are charging some of the Western men
with both dishonesty and disloyalty and
there is a general desire among members
cf the Amalgamated Association here for
eome form of inquiry.
The Chartiers plant at Carnegie is
owned by the American Sheet Steel Com
pany. The orders to tear It down went
out this afternoon. It will be removed to
Leechbnrg, in the Kiskiminetas Valley.
The strikers were called together yester
cay by Superintendent John Kenry and
preliminary notice of the company's plan
served on them. Eighty strikers respond
ed, and Mr. Henry told them that the
fires would be lighted and all preparations
made for a start to-day. He said that at
2 o'clock the whistle would be blown and
IX the men failed to respond the fires
would never be relighted. The programme
as outlined was carried out, but when the
whistle sounded the men did not respond.
The fires were at once drawn and the
order to dismantle and remove the mills
was given. The officials of the company
said that the plant would be down and
moved within two weeks.
Removing Another Plant.
So far good order has prevailed, al
though the steel officials assert that there
has been intimidation at Wheeling, Mc-
Keesport and Pittsburg. Both sides ex
press their confidence of ultimate success
and are carrying forward their plans.
President Shaffer of the Amalgamated As
sociation will leave to-morrow for Wheel-
Ing. Trhere be Is to address the strikers.
The number of men out as a result of the
general order is now roughly estimated at
16,000. and on the three orders at 62.000.
Many strikers have found work in inde
pendent mills or at other occupations and
many are idle merely because the skilled
men are out.
The strikers made gains at Wheeling
and the steel officials admitted to-day that
Benwood would be closed down." Clark's
mill here is moving along full-handed, the
liindsey & McCutcheon Js operating: wita
a 6mall crew and the managers are plan
ning to open Painter's and Increase the
force at Lindscy & McCutcheon's. A break
in the ranks of the men who struck at
Painter's Is expected, but the strike man
agers deny that one will occur. Another
break is reported as possible at New
castle, but there are as yet no positive
Indications of It. The American Tin Plate
Company has a small force at the Cres
cent plant in Cleveland and is also plan
ning resumption at other points with non
union men. The steel corporation has or
dered that the Chartiers plant at Carnegie
be dismantled and removed to Leechburp.
Make Gains at Wheeling:.
The Bellalre plant, in which the men
Etruck on Sunday, was finally closed down
to-day, working short-handed until yes
terday afternoon. When another start
will be made is problematical. Several
hundred boys employed at the National
Tube Works at McKeesport went out dur
ing the night and day and their action
materially aided the effort of the strikers
to finally .tie up the plant. The works o're
going ahead short-handed, but the ranks
of the workers are thinning and the sup
ply of material is limited.
Ten men, five of them skilled, quit at
the lower union mill of the Carnegie Com
pany in Pittsburg and joined the strikers.
Their defection represented the first break
in the Carnegie forces of more than 15,000
men.

Secretary of Dental Examiners' Board
in Chicago Arrested on Serious
Charges.
CHICAGO, Aug. 13.â Dr. Jacob H. Smy
ser, secretary of the State Board of Den
tal-Examiners, was arrested to-night on
two warrants issued by Judge Kavanagh,
charging him with forgery and with Issu
ing forged reports for the State board.
The arrest was made at the instance of
James H. Worman, United States Consul
at Munich, Bavaria, and brought to a cli
max his charges against the State board,
which caused Governor Yates to demand
the resignation of the members of the
body. After the arrest Worman declared
with emphasis that it was but the tlrst
step In what would be "one of the hottest
sensations in several years in Chicago."
Smyser was released on $3000 bail.
HONOLULU, Aug.' 6.â J. J. Dunne, act
ing United States District Attorney, has
written to Washington for instructions as
to the enforcement of the clause In tha
organic act of Hawaii, which states that
no corporation in the islands shall hold
and acquire more than 1000 acres of land.
The matter was brought up In a "suit of
J. B. Atherton against the "Wahiawa Su
gar Company, the defendant making a
claim that Atherton had no standing in a
court of equity, as he was violating, as
trustee of a corporation, the provision re
ferred to. This plea was overruled by
Judge Gear as having nothing to do with
the case at bar, but he called the atten
tion of the United S-jates District Attor
ney to the matter. Dunne secured a post
ponement of the case for thirty day3, un
til he could hear from Washington.
There arc many corporations here that
held thousands of acres, and the reply
frcm Washington is awaited with much
interest. Judge Gear held that the titles
of such corporations to any amount ot
land were valid, except against the Gov
ernment.
Increase in France's Population.
PARIS, Aug. 13.â The official census re
turns show the population of France to be
38.641.333, an Increase in the last five years
of 421,364. The increase Is mainly In urban
centers. Twenty-eight departments show
an increase and fifty-nine a decrease. The
census figures include the troops in China
and the sailors of the navy and merchant
marine absent from France March. 24.

he will sail for Europe to inspect the Co
lombian consulates of European countries.
He Is convinced that if his Government
considered the situation very critical they
would not3enJ him away and leave no
representative in Washington.

Senor Herran. Chafge d' Affaires of Co
lombia here, is inclined to believe that
there is some mistake about the report
that Dr. Sanclemente's friends are.con
tributing materially to the support of the
Insurrection. He received cable messages
from Colombia until July 27. since when
cable communication has been interrupt
ed, and his dispatches of much later, date
than July 8 contain no information con
firming mail advices received by the State
Department: On the contrary, up to the
time when cable communication ceased all
of Senor Herran's advices tended to show
that quiet was being restored throughout
Colombia and that only scattered guer
rilla bands were in the field in oppositidn
to the Government.' - ; j
Direct news from General Uribe was ex
pected a week ago, but it did not come.
His friends were disappointed again to
day, when the steamer Zuiia of the Red
D line arrived without bringing a letter
from him. Dr. Restrepo said to-night that
he did not believe the report of General
Uribe's death, and hoped to be in a posi
tion to give positive evidence in a day or
two that he Is alive and fighting.
Senor Eduardb. Espinoza, ex-Consui
General of Colombia in this city, also dis
credited the storr; . / J
No direct news has been received from
General Uribe since June 15, when,- it is
said, he sailed from this city for Venezu
ela. Colombian officials said that he went
there to secure aid from President Castro
In- the shape of arms and ammunition for
the rebellion. Since then Dr. A. J.
Restrepo, the diplomatic representative
of the insurgents, has received indirect
information that Uribe reached Colombia
safely and is fighting at the head of 15.000
men. ' Â¦ â¢
Much excitement was caused among
Colombians here by the report. The
friends of General Uribe consider it.sig
nificant that the Colombian Government
has not sent the news by cable to its rep
resentatives, for the Colombian Consulate
here has not been officially notified. While
there is nothing definite on which to base
a denial of the report, the general's
friends discredit it on the ground that
several times during the rebellion a year
ago General Uribe was -declared killed in
battle.

NOTEâ In'the above' exposition of the tidea
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time of
day: the third time column gives the last tide
of the day. except when there are but three
tides., as sometimes occurs. The heights given
are In addition to the soundings on the United
States Coast Survey charts, except when a
minus (â ) sign precedes the height, and then
the number given Is subtracted from the depth
given by the charts. The plane of reference 13
the mean of the lower, low waters.

The transport Sumner will probably sail
again for AlanMa September 1. ' She cume
here on a special order to bring up con
valescent soldiers, fand the chances are
that when she. gets back to the Philip

Meade is progressing rapidly and she will
be ready to sail on time next Friday. She
has a full supply of coal in her bunkers,
and will go out as well provided for as
any transport that has sailed during the
past six months.

DEXTSON, Tex., Aug. 13.â J. B. Davis;
Deputy Marshal of Colbert, I. TV arrived
here to-night from the scene of the rob
bery at" Caney. " *? v
"The following men have been arrested
on the^ charge of having committed the
robbery," said Davis: "E. C. Richmond.
Bob Alford, George Brown. John Gibson,
Tom Edwards and Jack Barr. These men
were arrested at Caney and taken to
Aloka this morning. The operator at
Caney. when he heard the shooting, sus
pected the train was being- held up and put
out the light in the station and telegraph
ed to Atoka, eleven miles away, for of
ficers. Within two hours after the rob
bery was committed bloodhounds had
trailed these men to their homes. We
found wet clothing: identified by passen
gers as that worn by the robbers. We
found three masks and found In the fire
box of the cookstove when the arrests
were made the shoes of the. men arrested
The tracks were measured and fitted the
shoes exactly."
This afternoon the men were given a
hearing In Commissioner Ball's court at
Atoka and their bonds fixed at $3000 in
each case. They could not give the bonds
and were remanded to jail.
There, were two men connected with
the robbery who rode away -on horse
back and have not been captured. Postal
Clerk Georgre E. Tuley this evening fin
ished straightening out the registered
mail taken out of the pouches and said
"There Is practically no loss on the reg
istered packages. There was a consign
ment of $4000 worth of stamps for the
postofflce at Sherman in the pouches that
were sacked, but these the robbers did
not take."

Loses a Race by Fouling.
CHICAGO. Aug. 13.â William H. Thomp
son, the Cadillac's skipper, presented the
Invader with the third race of the inter
national yachting match for the Canada's
cup by fouling the Canadian boat at the
6tart, in full view of the judges and most
of the spectators. Captain A. Jarvis of
the Invader declined to .fly his protest
flag, but the judges steamed after the
Cadillac and notified Captain. Thompson
that they disqualified his boat, whereupon
the Cadillac left the race. The Invader
sailed over the course. Judge "Warner
epeaklng officially, gave It out that the in
cident was Captain Thompson's, own
fault and that the judges had no course
left open to them but to disqualify the
defender.

elry. Both safes were demolished by the
explosion and the car is a wreck.
The robbers then put a charge of dyna
mite to the safe and 'blew it open. They

$150, as the money had all been left at
Muskogee, to ie picked up by the "Katy
Flyer," which runs through the Territory
by day. The passengers and clerk were
forced to assist the bandits in their work.
Every mail pouch was emptied and the
mail " considered valuable by them was
taken.
Bobbery of the Passengers.
Dynamite "Wrecks a Car.
From -within came no resiÂ»onse. The
two men could not be seen. They waited
only the result. When there -sras no reply
from within the robbers again called out:
"Open the door or we will blow it open
and blow you to hell."
There was no response to _tne second
call, and the firing again began, and fully
twenty shots were discharged. Still there
was no response to the firing and three
men prepared a smaJl blast of dynamite
against the -car door.
In the meantime the shooting*had awak
ened the passengers. The first intimation
that it was a hold-up sent the conductor,
brakeman and porter back into the train,
and as they came in and called out "It's a.
hold-up," the passengers tried to hide
their money and valuables. The dynamite
blast was exploded. It was a small
charge and was intended more to frighten
than a-nything- else.
A larger blaet of dynamite was pre
pared and exploded. This did the work.
It tore the side off the car. The robbers
leaped through the opening and the
frightened messenger had nothing to do
but accept their orders. He was over
powered and narrowly escaped being torn
in shreds, as was his car. He was ordered
to open the safe, but he could not, as the
combination was .at .the end of the run.

The Tampico was built last year by the
Craig Shipbuilding- Company of Toledo,
Ohio, and is owned by the Hawgood Tran
sit Company of Cleveland, Ohio. She is
1451 tons net burden, 247 feet long, 42 feet
â¢beam and 24 feet de'ep. She will probably,
go into the local coal trade.
The Tampico took 102 days to make the
run.- She was caught ir. a big storm off
the River Platte and had to put into Mon
tevideo with her rudder damaged. She ar
rived there on June 11 and did not get
away again until June 28. The run
through the Straits" of Magellan 'and up
the Pacific Coast was uneventful. The
Tampico brings 1900 tons of coal, con
signed to the United States Government.
The new American steamship Tampico
arrived from Norfolk,- Va., yesterday.' She
is one of a ileet of ten vessels Â¦ coming
here, to go into the coast^trade. Of the
ones that have already got here the Asun
cion is in the coal trade and the Enter
prise has been purchased for the Hilo run.

Tne Argus belongs to the California
Navigation and Improvement Company
and was brought down here about two
weeks ago to load shingles. She was par
tially loaded on the Mission mudflats, and
when last seen Monday night seemed se
curely moored. From some unknown
cause, however, the mooring line parted
and then the anchor dragged. Once in
deep water the anchor was off the bottom
and' the Argus went out with th'e tide.
The- Snow & Burgess arrived from the
Sound with lumber over a week ago, and
she is awaiting a chance to load.
The big river barge Argus, partially
loaded with shingles, was nearly wrecked
on Alcatraz last Monday night. She was
anchored in. Mission Bay, but broke adrift,
and went out with the tide. Off Vallejo
street she collided with the bark Snow &
Burgess and started a number of planks
in that vessel's -hull. Eefore the crew of
the barlccould secure her the Argus broke
away and soon disappeared in the fog.
Early yesterday morning the Spreckels
Towboat Company was notified that a
barge was ashore on Alcatraz, and the
tug Reliance â¢ ivas sent to tow her off.
When Captain Shaw got to the island he
found the barge hard and fast on the
beach directly I'nder the prison. A sol
dier was standing: guard on the cliff, and
as soon as the tug was made fast a cor
poral's guard went aboard the barge and
searched her for possible runaways.. Soon
after the Reliance started pulling on her
the Argus came off and was towed over
to the Pacific-street wharf.

LUMBER BAEGE ADRIFT.
Soon after the Encinal picked up the
men the launch " William D came along
and made fast to the derelict. The sloop
would not tow, however, and soon after
she seemed to disappear altogether. The
boatmen think that she will wash up on
Ooat Island. t
very narrow escape from drown
ing yesterday. Had.it not been for the
prompt assistance rendered by Captain
Harmsworth of the ferry steamer Encinal
all of them would most likely have been
drowned.
When' the ferry-boat was about ten
minutes out from Oakland mole Captain
Harmsworth saw a boat bottom up with
three men clinging to it. He at once al
tered the Encinal's course and stood over
toward the lighthouse on Goat Island.
When close enough the â¢ steamer was
stopped and a boat lowered. It took some
little time to get the men off the capsized
boat, as they could not help themselves,
and the men in the lifeboat were afraid of
drowning some of them. Finally they
were got aboard the Encinal and after
being given a restorative were sent into
the ttreroom to dry their clothes.
The men say the accident" was due to
carrving too much sail and too little bal
last. "We started out from Oakland
Creek on a iishing expedition in the thir
ty-ton sloop Pearl this morning,"- said
Coleman. "We sailed around Goat Island,
anchoring at different places, and made
a very good eatch. Finally it began to
blow and the sea got choppy. Then we
decided. to go home, and after heaving up
the anchor made a start. A few minutes
after we got under way a squall struck
the Pearl and she turned clean over.-
Luckily we got clear of her and managed
to clamber back on the keel. We could
not keep our place there, however, as
sometimes she would roll one way antl
sometimes another. Kach roll was enough
to throw us off, and then we would have
to clamber back again. This kind of work
in chilly -water soon tired us, and I think
in another ten minutes we would have
been drowned if the Encinal had not come
along. I suppose the sloop will be recov
ered, but even if she is not we ought to
be grateful that we are alive."

Three Americans to Suf
fer for a Raid in
China.
Wins Santa Rosa Har

Gypsy Boy, David S and

Special Dispatch to The CaU.
VICTORIA, B. C, Aug. 13.â The steam
er Olympia, which arrived from, the Or
ient this morning, brings advices of the
trial of three Americans by United States
Consul Ragsdale at Tientsin. They were
charged with looting in Chankoechuang,
a Chinese village thirty miles northwest
of Peking. The accused â Richard Leslie
Butler, Alfred Plant and James "Winnâ
were sentenced to four years' imprison
ment each.
According to the complaint of Chang
Tuen Fang, which was made to the Amer
ican Charge d' Affaires at Peking by Li
Hung Chang under date of July 8. the
three Americans, together with H. F.
Piper, correspondent of a Shanghai paper,
and others, took a number of Chinese
carts to the Chinese village to loot tha
place on June 23. They broke into the / j
Chinese pawnshop and, firing a. fusillade '*
which frightened the Chinese, declared
the pawnbroker was accused of being a
Boxer leader and that they had come to
search the place for firearms. They strung
him up and bound all those in the store>
and, threatening further violence, de
manded payment of 5000 taels. The Chi
nese could only raise but 500 taels. Not
satisfied, they ransacked the place, keep-
Ing up a constant gun fire, in which one
man was wounded. While they were ri
fling the place a Chinese military patrol
approached the row of Chinese carts
brought by the looters and>the drivers,
becoming frightened, fled. They were fol
lowed by the soldiers, who captured one.
The Americans spent the night in thÂ»
pawnshop and next morning, just before
daylight, they left with a large quantity
of looted silver and jewelry. They seized
the pawnbroker's brother and made him
walk in front of them as a hostage, to
prevent the soldiers firing on the party.
They were subsequently arrested because
of a complaint laid in Peking and will
serve four years' imprisonment for their
offense.
SANTA ROSA. Aug. 13.â A largely In
creased attendance over that of yester
day marked- the second day of the race
meeting of the Santa Rosa Association.
It was another case of good day and fast
track, ajid some fine sport was witnessed.
First on the card was a trot against time.
The Santa Rosa stock farm's bay mare
Lady Russell, with George Ramage up,
starting to .beat* 2:31. She finished easily
in 2:26^4. Another of Pierce's horses, In
ferno, a chestnut stallion, started to beat
a mark of 2:26, and Ramage drove him
under the wire In 2^14Vi.'
The first event of the day was a 2:25
pace, for which a purse of $500 had been
hung up. It was best three out of five,
mile heats, and four entries responded to
the bell. Charlie Whitehead's sorrel geld
ing Toppy~ sold as a heavy favorite in the
betting, the bookies finally offering 6 to
1 against the field with no takers. Toppy
Justified the expectations of his backers
by winning In straight heats in 2:15%,
2:WA and 2:18%.
The second race was a running event,
five furlongs," with six entries.. S. F.
Capps' bay colt Gypsy Boy, with Burllng
ame in the saddle, took the lead and
showed a clean pair of heels all the way,
coming under the wire in 1:03.
In the third race, also a running event
at six furlongs, McLaughlin's Mike Rice
seemed to be a gen'eral favorite, but Ed
Corey's David S proved the winner In
1:14 at even money.
The fourth and last race of the day, a
five furlong dash, went to John "Whelan's
speedy little mare Swiftwater in 1:01^.

Single-Scullers, Swimmers and Other
Athletes Will Go North From
i San Francisco.
The programme of the eighth annual
Astoria regatta has been sent down to San
Francisco by J. C." McCue, secretary of
the executive committee, and includes the
following events: Pacific Coast single
shell championship; consolation single
shell race; four-oared shell race; four
oared barge race; junior outrigged skin!
race; senior outrigged skiff race; single
pleasure boat race, and double pleasure
boat race. The single shell, four-oared
shell, four-oared barge and outrigged skiff
(senior-and junior) races will probably be
rowed off in heats. In the four-oared shell
race there will be four competitorsâ the
Portland, Victoria and Vancouver, B. C,
and' the University of California crews;
There will a(Â§p be four barge crews, rep
resenting the Alameda and Olympic Boat
clubs and the South End and Ariel Row
ing clubs. â¢
The Astorians expect Alex '"W. Pape of
the Dolphins to go up and defend his title
aa senior shell champion of the Pacific
Coast against the northern scullers, but It
is not probable that he will make the trip
this year. It is also expected that repre
sentatives of the Ariel. Alameda and
South End clubs will pull in the single
shell races. The prizes in all the rowing
events will be handsome gold medals.
In addition toMhe rowing events there
will be duck hunts, tub races, walking the
greasy pole, races in Chinese barges, div
ing and swimming under water, life-sav
ing exhibitions, high diving, swimming
and races in gigs between crews of the
United States vessels Philadelphia, Man
zanita and Columbine. The rowing races
will take place"on the mornings of August
29 and 30 and the sailing races In the aft
ernoons of the same two days. On. the
morning of the first day there will also be
a race between gasoline launches for the
cup presented by the" Union Gas. Engine
Company of San Francisco. On the after
noon of Saturday, August 31. the track
and field sports will be held, in which
teams will take , part â¢ representing the
Olympic Club of San Francisco, the
Multnomah Amateur.- Athletic Club and
the Young Men's Christian Association
clubs of Portland," the University of Ore
gon, Pacific University and Spokane.
Teams from Seattle and Tacoma may also
compete. . '
The coronation of the queen of the
carnival and the ball in her honor will
take place on the'nlght of August 29, and
on the next evening there will be an In
door athletic- exhibition, the principal fea
ture being a* wrestling- match between Mc-
Kenzie of the Olympic Club and Calm of
the Multnomah Athletic Club. There will
also be boxing, club swinging, tumbling,
juggling, exhibitions on the slack wire,
horizontal bar and flying rings, bag
punching and comic acrobatic feats. The
three days of the regatta will be pre
ceded by a street fair, in which will be
seen the people of the Elks\ carnival at
Tacoma. â¢-â¢ Â¦.,' Â¦
Altogether* if the strike does not pre
vent the sailing of the steamers for
Portland, transportation will be pro
vided by the carnival committee for thirty
athletes from San Francisco. . Â»
Mining Engineer Bobbed.
Frank Vadls, a mining engineer, who re
sides at 139 Park street. Alameda, claims
to have been assaulted and robbed by a
mob of about twenty-five men near Mis
sion and First streets last evening. Vadis
says he was approached - by a crowd,
called a "teamster" and beaten.' Some
one struck . hint from behind, and while
partly insensible his pockets were rifled
and $40 in coin, a sight draft for ?300 and

1HO ACTIVITY ON THE FRONT.
It is probable a delegation from the
Labor Council V.-I11 make a further formal
protest to-night before the Board of Po
lice Commissioners against the appoint
ment of additional special police officers.
"\Ve are more interested in this letter
which we have just received from Mayor
Phelan," said Secretary Rosenberg. "It is
in response to the conference we had with
him yesterday, and we will probably reply
to it to-day. For the present we have
nothing to say."
Nothing v.-as heard of the proposition at
the headquarters of the Labor Council,
where the executive committee .was in
session until a late hour, and none of the
members would express an opinion re
garding what steps would be taken in
case the proposition should be presented
to them.
Efforts to bring- together all those inter
ested in arranging: a plan of conciliation
have resulted in the suggestion that per
haps Benjamin Ide Wheeier, president of
the University of California, might prove
an agreeable mediator to both the labor
ing element ani the Employers' Associa
tion. President "Wheeler was in the city
yesterday afternoon and said he would
act if both side-; would agree to the prop
osition, but would not endeavor to force
himself into the controversy. The matter
will be presented to the Employers' Asso
ciation and the San Francisco Labor
Council to-day.
At the headquarters of tho City Front
Federation, the San Francisco Labor
Council and the Building Trades Council
the leaders in the strike movement were
busy catching up with neglected details,
and r.o move of consequence was made.
The rest seemed very welcome and much
accumulated business was disposed of.

ters cf the Draymen': Association was
closed, and only a very few teams. work-
Ing under police escort and hauling per
ishable goods, were in evidence.

SAN BERNARDINO, Aug. 13.â George
B. Coffin, a well-known resident of Red
lands, died yesterday, morning while rid
ing with a lady friend. He was suddenly
attacked by paralysis of the heart and
before anything could be done for him he
fell a corpse into the arms of his friend.
When Coffin left home he was apparently
in fairly good health. Scarcely any warn
ing of the approaching tragedy was given.
He gasped once or twice and, then fell
forward. His friend supported his body
until she received assistance.
Falls Dead in His Carriage.
What promised to be a bloody battle be
tween the police and the strikers was pre
vented on the water front yesterday by
the liberal use of clubs by Officers Mc-
Grath and McCormack. Sergeant Cook at
tempted to disperse a gang of man that
were hanging around the neighborhood of
the strikers' headquarters and succeeded
in driving them all away with one excep
tion. William Herley resisted the at
tempts of- the sergeant and gave him bat
tle. He struck at Cook several times and
then threw his arms around him and tried
to throw him. The men who had been
driven away came running back, and for
a few moments trouble seemed imminent
Officers McGrath and McCormack, who
were on duty at the Mission street whart
ran to the rescue of Sergeant Cook and
by using their clubs freely kept the men
back. An opportunity offered and Mc-
Grath brought his heavy stick down on
Herley's head, rendering him unconscious
Herley was taken to the Emergency Hos
pital/where his wounds, which consisted
of lacerations of the head, were dressed
Sergeant Cook escaped without injury*
John Burns, who attempted to aid Her
ley, was arrested by Officer McCormack
Sergeant Cook Assaulted.

Secretary Lytle of the Master Painters'
Association claims that but two of ' its
members have signed the schedule. "The
majority of the employing painters who
have signed the agreement," he said, "are
small concerns that do not belong to the
association. They are bosses one day and
journeymen the next."
"The Building Trades Council at the
present time Is In possession of facts
which show that the employing painters
of this city and county, using 90 per cent
of the material are agreed to the request
of the council, and those of them who
have not up to the present time opened
up their shops are In many instances now
prepared to do so, and will in the future,
as in the past, resume harmonious rela
tions with their employes.- There are
ninety-eight shops operating at the pres
ent time, many of whom belong to the
Master Painters' Association and all of
which are in accord with the request of
this council."
According to P. H. . McCarthy, president
of the Building Trades Council, the strike

Work on the. streets was suspended yes
terday by reason of the day being a mu
nicipal legal holiday. The city wagons
and sprinklers lay idle all day in. the Cor
poration yards, as the inspectors of the
Bureau of Streets of the Board of Public

work with a full force next Monday.

A delegation of tanners from San Mateo
County is expected to attend the meeting
to-night of the local Tanners' Union. Re
cently the tanners in Redwood City and
San Mateo became affiliated with the San
Franci6co Labor Council and with
local Tanners' Union No. 9018. The
grievance of the tanners is that schooner
loads of tanbark handled by non-union
crews have been landed in San Mateo
County and the men refused to handle it.
They walked out and are coming to ask
the support of their brethren in this city
In their struggle. About 100 men will be
affected by this action. -

James Maddison, a marine engineer, liv
ing at East and Mission streets, swore to
a complaint before Judge Cabaniss yes
terday morning charging Policeman O. H.
Knight with battery. He said that he was
standing on East street Monday when two
or three strikers were fighting and a
mounted policeman. rode along and com
pelled them to fall back to the sidewalk.
He was crowded off. the sidewalk, when
Knight came ud to him and told him to
move on. He said he was walking as fast
as he could, whereupon Knight struck
him on the head with his club. Knight
surrendered himself at the City Prison
and was released on $10 cash bail. Knight
said that nearly 200 strikers were congre
gated in front of the entrance of the Mall
dock and got up a fight among them
selves to distract his attention so that
they could rush the gates and do violence
to the non-union men at work. Policeman
Meyers came along on horseback and the
policemen started in to clear the street of
the strikers. Maddison defied Knight to
make him move and the latter gave him
a shove. Maddison then called him an
offensive name and again defied him, when
he drew his club and hit him on the head
with it. In a few minutes the street was
cleared.

Sailors' Union that a crew be permitted
to discharge the brig W. G. Irwin, which
is tied up at the Puget Sound Lumber
Company's wharf with a deckload of lum
ber and a hold full of lime. The owners
fear the lime will slack. The union re
fused the request, but was willing to fur
nish men to keep the brig pumped out in
case the vessel began to leak.
The Laundry Workers' Union has pre

measure is intended to prevent the men
there employed from working all night.

hausted.

STRIKE IN OAKLAND.
REDONDO BEACH, Aug. 13.â The
steamer Santa Rosa arrived at this port
from San Francisco last night a little
more than an hour behind schedule time.
The Santa Rosa is the only steamer that
has touched at Redondo in a week. She
brought the usual number of passengers
and about 200 tons of freight. To guard
against the possibility of disaffection
among the boat's crew a high wooden
barricade was run across the shore end
of the pier and guarded by local peace
officers, who prevented every one passing
eKcept those officially connected with the
boat or engaged in moving its cargo.
The Pacific Coast Steamship Company
has been making extraordinary efforts 10
prevent a complete tie-up of its boats.
In order to do this the company has cen
tralized its forces on the Santa Rosa
lesterday the boat carried a crew of
thirty-one firemen and coal passers in
stead of the regular quota of eighteen
All of these men were paid $6 a day for
the service.

San Francisco, Aug. 13, 1901.
There is one point I desire the Labor Council to understand
and appreciateâ that whatever may be the merits of the con
troversy between the employers and the employes, I, as a law
officer, must see that legal rights are preserved and that In
the pursuit of them no man shall suffer violence if it may be
prevented.
This is the authority under which the Commissioners have
acted. The Chief will swear in no man for such service unless
after an examination he proves himself to be of good charac
ter. He shall be instructed in his duty by the Chief, who will
see that he does not exceed his instructions.
"The Board of Police Commissioners shall have power, at
its discretion, upon the petition of any person, firm or corpora
tion, to appoint, and at pleasure to remove, special police offi
cers. Such officers shall be subject to all of the rules and regu
lations of the board."
It was therefore a question for the Board of Police Com
missioners to decide whether the best interests of the city
would be served by granting' or refusing this request, 'ine
board decided several weeks ago that, as special officers, such
men were amenable to its rules and regulations, but as private
detectives or guards they would be wholly under the control
of their employers, ana for this reason the public interests
eeemed to dictate that they be put under the control of and made
responsible to the constituted authorities. This was the only
reason which prompted the decision of the Commissioners.
The charter, section 4, chapter III, article VIII, provides as
f ollows :
same privilege to others. Now, as a matter of fact, on account
of the excited condition of the public mind, many acts.of law
lessness have been reported here and there throughout the city
in neighborhoods which have had no police protection. There
fore the present plan of the Police Commission is to take the
regular police off the trucks and assign them to their 'beats.
When apprised of this the owners of drays decided to employ
gruards or detectives at their own expense to protect their
property and employes, and they furthermore asked that these
men be made special officers as authorized by law.
The Labor Council, In common with other labor organiza
tions, has declared its policy to preserve peace and order and
cf acting strictly within its legal rights while granting the
From my observation on that occasion I must say that I
fully acquit Mr. Newhall of any discourtesy or of any prejudice
or bias In the matter of which you complain. On this account
and for other reasons I am of the opinion that he will act
fairly and impartially as a member of the Board of Police
Commissioners according to his oath of office, which is to sup
port and defend the laws.
When by appointment I personally met the board they
promptly and formally refused to discuss the subject with me,
and as they preferred to remain in Ignorance of the real condi
tions I did not place much value on their previous expression
of opinion.
After receiving the communication I asked the privilege
from the officers of meeting- the board of directors that after
noon for the purpose of considering strike conditions, which
Â¦was granted. I then called upon the Governor and asked him
to attend the meeting with me and to Jointly propose a plan
for the Chamber of Commerce, as a body peculiarly Interested,
to advance a means of settling the strike. I did not ask the
Governor directly or indirectly to take any other action.
I would first call your attention to the fact that the letter
was not the letter of Mr. Newhall, but a communication from
the beard of directors of the Chamber of Commerce and so
signed* Messrs. Newhall and Scott certifying: as officers to its
authenticity as the act of the board. While I believe that the
communication was unwise and unnecessary, still I have re
garded It simply as an expression of opinion from the board of
directors of that body.

Somebody ought to make a dash for the
North. Pole now and find it. It is hot as
far north as the wires run.
So many new and annoying bugs have made their
appearance in. the East lately that naturalists have
come to the conclusion that in killing off various
kinds of "vermin" civilized man has destroyed the
balance of nature and is now suffering the conse
quences.

that such isolation is impracticable. The Advocate

The Advocate says that we have taken too seriously

point in The Call's contention to which we give our

the army canteen. But we are not in favor-of adding

TEMPERANCE FANATICISM.
A Connecticut man went to jail the other day rather
than pay a license for the obnoxious freedom of his
dog. He may have shared but would not admit the
opinion of the rest of us that the genus dog is ob
jectionable enough to be suppressed on general prin
ciples. ;Â£r '-â¢/Â¦v.
PROPERTYâ J. M. S. C. City. If a
man marries a widow ard after marriage
purchases property with his individual
money, then it Is his individual or sepa
rate property and in no wise community
property. If the mother should die flrat
and she before death give to her children
one-half of the property she owned be
fore marriage, that would be perfectly
legal. The children would not have any
rights in the separate property of their
stepfather.
AFFIDAVITâ S.. City. When a person
desires to enter a tract of United States
land, he may appear personally at the dis
trict land office and present his applica
tion and make the required affidavit be
fore the register or receiver. He may.
however, make the affidavit before tho
Judge or clerk of a court of record or be
fore a Commissioner of the United States
District Court and save the journey ta
the Land Office. Â¦ ,\

Saul Epsteen, a San Francisco boy.
graduate of the Lowell High School and
University of California, has just received
his degree of doctor of philosophy at {he
Â¦University . of Zurich, Switzerland. He
left for Zurich a year ago. The usual
course is three years, but owing to Ep
steen's unusually fine thesis the authori
ties of the university made an exception
in his case and graduated him in one
year. _

A CHANCE TO SMILE.
ANSWERS TO QUERIES-
PERSONAL MENTION.
.These items show something more than a popular
interest in wireless telegraphy and the eagerness of
inventors to improve upon the system. of Marconi.
They prove that the Marconi system is no longer an
experiment merely, but an accomplished invention of
use to the world and of such importance that capital
is ready to support almost any well devised enterprise
directed to its operation: It is well known that in
ventions do; not come singly," and there may soon be
as many kinds of wireless telegraphy as there are
sewing machines or harvesters, f In fact, now that the
primary difficulty in the- way of sending messages
without wires has been overcome there is no reason
why improvements should not be both rapid and nu
merous. In the course, of the development of the art
the Marconi system will be superse'ded, but nothing
can take away from the gifted Italian the credit
belongs to the first inventor of that method of tele
graphing. He has laid the- foundation. Others can
do.no more tljan build upon it.
Another dispatch in the same paper and of the
same date announces the completion of a plant for
wireless telegraphy at -Siasconset, Mass. The New
York Times about the same time reports arrange
ments madeÂ» for an "improved wireless telegraphy"
between Boston and Danvers, where an electrical and
telegraph school has bought a farm of sufficient. area
for running electric cars in connection with a- course
of instruction for motormen. It is stated that a relay
system for intercepting a wireless message and send
ing it forward with more^ force has been invented by
the principal of the school, and he expects to prove
its efficiency in the operation of the line he has just
established. The claims made for the invention sound
extravagant, for it is said: "The inventor professes
to be able to send a message" fifty miles over land and
then pass it through the relay apparatus, which he can
manipulate *at the starting point. He predicts that
such stations can be established at intervals of about
fifty miles, repeating and forwarding the message al
most indefinitely." ' â¢
RECENTLY the New York Sun announced that
there has been established near Port Jefferson,
Long Island, a large plant designed to operate
a wireless telegraph system devised by Nikola Tesla.
For the purposes of the plant 200 acres of ground have
been purchased. ThÂ« main building will be 100 feet
square and will be occupied by an electric dynamo
with a capacity of 350- horsepower. It is the expec
tation of the managers of the plant that from that
point it will be possible to carry on wireless teleg
raphy over a wide region of country extending from
Philadelphia to Boston.

. The Vice President has rather increased public in
terest in him as a character unique among our pub
lic men.
Now he has gone back there as the guest of the
State to deliver the address at the celebration of the
quarter centennial of Colorado's admission. He has
revisited Victor and other scenes of the riotous
demonstrations against him last fall, and his clear
grit and good humor have won the people over. It
may not be charged that he does this for political
effect. It is simply the act of a strong and very self
centered man, to show that he is not capable of fear
and has confidence in his countrymen's sense of fair
play. Whether Colorado know it or not, he has ren
dered that State a great service by his return. He
has permitted her to purge her reputation and remove
the stain placed upon her by the mobs of last year.
Had he not returned to the scenes of those excesses
Colorado must have rested under the stigma placed
by her own disorderly people.
At one place a fellow bawled: "How about em
balmed beef?" Instantly the plucky colonel an
swered, "I ate it, and took army fare, while you are
too big a coward to serve your country." He re
fused to hasten his departure from towns when he was
threatened with, violence, and would not leave a hall
by a back door nor take a side street to his train
in the interest of his personal safety. The public au
thorities connived at the attacks made upon him, but
he stayed his schedule time in Colorado, and the first
outing he took after his election was in Colorado to
hunt mountain lions.
ROOSEVELT may excite antagonism, as posi
" tive men do, but he is no quitter. His progress
through Colorado during the campaign was
through an almost continuous mob. He was at times
restrained with difficulty from accepting the challenge
and going into a hand to hand fight with his torment
ors. He refused to quit speaking when interrupted
and gave back as good as the mob sent
â¢ ROOSEVELT'S GRIT.
If all stories are true that were told at the time the
various "punitive expeditions" sent out by Walder
see were in effect as brutal and as cruel as the
slaughter on the banks of the Amur, but the slaugh
tering done during the expeditions was carried out
over a wide region of country and extended over a
considerable period of time, and consequently there
was no signal dramatic horror to rouse public at
tention throughout the civilized world and stagger
humanity. Therefore, while Gribski dies a suicide,
Count Waldersee is received with honor by an Em
peror who cordially indorses aH he did, and, indeed
accepts the credit of having done it himself. It is th'e
fortune of v/ar.
Russian news is very carefully censored and it is
not certain what causes led Gribski to commit sui
cide, but it is believed the Czar held him responsible
for the cruel slaughtering of a number of peaceable
Chinese who were living on the Russian side of the
Amur at the time of the Boxer outbreaks. The
story goes that when the Chinese attacked the city
of Blagovestchensk Gribski asked instructions as to
v,hat he should do with the Chinese in the town, and
was ordered to "fling the Chinese across the river."
The general was absent when the dispatch came, but
his subordinate interpreted it literally and the entire
Chinese population was driven into the river. It will
be remembered that the massacre gave rise to a great
deal of comment at the time. Gribski was recalled to
St. Petersburg to make an explanation, and now the
story comes of his death by his own hand.
The two commanders seem to have had an equal
chance to advance their fortunes. Each served an
autocratic master capable of richly rewarding him,
and each went to the front resolved to do his mas
ter's bidding. The duty that awaited them was that
of reducing China to such a condition of weakness
that her Government would consent to pay any in
demnity asked by her foes and grant any other terms
they chose. Gribski failed. His management was
such that he was made the scapegoat for everything
that went wrong with the Russian forces, while Wal
dersee was so successful that he has felt at liberty to
say that everything he did was done in accord with the
will of the Kaiser," or, to put if in his own words: "If
I succeeded in some measure in China, it must be at
tributed solely to the fact that I had Emperor
Wilhelm behind me and was able to act in' accord
ance with his instructions."
BY an interesting coincidence of dates we re
ceive information of the honors conferred
upon Count Waldersee upon his arrival at
Berlin immediately following the announcement that
the General Gribski who commanded the Russian
forces on the Chinese frontier at the time of the Boxer
uprising has committed suicide. Gribski returned to
St. Petersburg to make a report even as Waldersee
returned to Berlin, but while the one found favor with
his sovereign the other met such a greeting as led
him to put an end to his life. .

A Des Moines couple who divorced themselves lived
in the same house for twenty years with nothing more
material than a chalk line on the floor to divide them.
They never crossed the barrier, and it is safe to say
that neither lost anything by their separation. There
are some things not worth encouragement.

NOTED PRELATE DUE IN NEW YORK TO-MORROW "WHO BEARS
GREETING FROM ENGLAND TO THE TRIENNIAL CONVENTION
OF THE EPISCOPAL CHURCH TO BE HELD HERE IN OCTOBER.
The Pan-American Congress has been called mainly
for the purpose of devising means of settling disputes
among the various states of this hemisphere by arbi
tration. Should it succeed in doing so it will fill a
long-felt want. . At the present time our Government
is gathering quite a fleet 'around the isthmus and
may soon have occasion to make use of it. A short
time ago a dispatch was received at Washington from,
our Consul General at Panama to the effect that the
Liberals had stopped a passenger-train at Matachin,
and as the United States is bound by treaty to keep
traffic by that route open to the world, the Govern
ment will have to interfere should such disturbances
of transportation become frequent or dangerous.
Altogether our Spanish brothers in the great pan-
American family are decidedly troublesome, and. if
they can devise a means of putting a stop to their
wars we will be quite willing to help them enforce it.
From the figures given the contending forces ap
pear to be large, and yet a war that has been drag
ging along for two years can hardly have been
prosecuted with much vigor. A recent dispatch an
nounced a battle in which 900 men were killed on the
Government side, but such battles are rare. As a
rule the only information that comes from the dis
turbed country is that of menace to property. The
passengers who arrived at New York a few days ago
on the steamer Altai reported that there is a des
perate condition of affairs in the interior of the coun
try. As a result of the revolt and the uncertainty of
the 1 future business is at a standstill and gold is said
to be at a premium of 3000 per cent. As if it did not
have trouble enough with its own people, the Colom
bian Government now charges Venezuela with giv
ing aid to the rebels and threatens war against that
country.
The trouble is the outcome of the seemingly inter
minable conflicts tHat go on in those countries be
tween the men who" are" in office and those who are
out. Diaz Guerrajflthe representative in New York
of the Colombian revolutionists, is, reported to have
said recently: ,'The revolution has lasted since
1899, and there have been killed or, wounded during
that time about 40,000 men. Only as recently as May
there was fought-at PalosNegros a bloody battle that
continued for seventeen days. At the end of that pe
riod the revolutionists had to retreat for want of am
munition, and the Government soldiers were too ex
hausted to follow them. At present the regulars
(Government troops) number some 20,000, and the
Liberals (revolutionists) outnumber them." .
FROM, the disturbed , condition . of affairs in
Venezuela and Colombia it appears that our
Government may have to interfere to protect the
interests of foreigners, or else give way and permit
Europe to do the work. The latter alternative is of
course not to be thought of, . and consequently our
Government will doubtless be forced "to prepare to
act vigorously at any /point in the vicinity of .the
Isthmus of Panama where disturbances are likely to
be injurious to property' and to foreign trade.. .

deliberate executive session with himself, has reached
the conclusion that heaven is excessively hot as a
place of residence. If this be the fact, it seems to fol
low that there will not be such an advantage for us
after all in a choice of abodes.

THE! most distinguished figure among
the remarkable men at the coming
triennial convention of the Episco
pal, church, which convenes In San
Francisco October 3, . will be the
Right Rev. Edgar Jacob, Lord Bishop of
Newcastle, England, a Peer of Great Brit
ain and a prelate of the highest attain
ments, who comes as the personal repre
sentative of his Grace the Archbishop of
Canterbury and of the whole English
episcopate as the bearer pt greetings from
the gTeat- church from which the Ameri
can Episcopal traces Its origin. Dr. Ja
cob is regarded as one of the ablest pre
lates of the British "church and Is a
preacher of acknowledged power. As
Bishop of a population largely Industrial,
his Influence Is very great. To his efforts
the final settlement of a desolating strike
among the coal men* of his diocese on a
basis satisfactory to both parties in con
troversy was due. The Bishop will be
heard during the convention at several
churches and at the great meeting in the
Mechanics' Pavilion. The Lord Bishop
left England on Saturday last and will
reach New York, It Is expected; on Thurs
day of this week.
The Right Rev. Edward Jacob, Lord
Bishop of Newcastle-on-Tyne, is the son
of the Venerable Philip Jacob, Arch
deacon . of Winchester, and was born in
1S44. He was educated at Winchester and
is a graduate of Oxford, where he ob
tained a, first class in classics and an A.
B. degree, taking a third class in the final
classical school in 1867. In 1870 the degree
,of M. A. was conferred, and by diploma
D. D. in 1895. In 1896 the honorary degree
of D. D. was conferred by Durham Uni
versity. He was ordained deacon In 1868
and priest In 1869. From 1868 to 1869 ho
was curate of Taynlow, in the diocese of
Oxford; curate of Witney to 1871,,curate
of Bermondsey In 1872 and domestic chap
lain of Bishop Mllman of Calcutta from
1872 to 1876; commissary of the Bishop of
Calcutta from 1876 to 1888, and until 1896
examining chaplain to the famous Harold
Browne, Lord Bishop of Winchester. He
was honorary canon of Winchester for
twenty years, honorary chaplain to the
Queen from 1884 to 1896, chaplain in or
dinary to the Queen from 1890 to 1895,
chaplain to Bishop Davidson of Winches
ter in 1895-96, rural dean of Landport and
chaplain to her Majesty's prison in King
ston, Portsmouth, from 1892 to 1896; proctor
in convocation for Hampshire and the
Isle of Wight from 1895 to 1896, honorable
secretary to the Board of Missions for
the province of Canterbury from 1888 to
189C. He was consecrated Lord Bishop of
Newcastle in succession to Dr. Wilber
force, translated to Chlchester In York
Minster on January 25, 1896. The title of
the see is Newcastle and the jurisdiction
extends over the city and county of New
castle, the county of Northumberland, the
It now remains to be seen what sort of a ticket he
will nominate. He may deem it advisable to place
at least some good men -on the ticket to give it
strength. WHen the ticket is made up it will then be
time to determine how far self-respecting men can
support it. That, however, is a question for the fu
tttre. The one thing for Republicans to do this morn
ing is to bow to Kelly and take their medicine. He
is the doctor.
For this condition cf affairs no one is to blame ex
cept the good citizens who were too busily engaged
in private affairs to attend to their public duties even
when such duty entailed no further energy than that
of going to the polls and casting a ballot.- Those
who stayed away from the polls virtually voted for
Kelly. The situation was made clearly known to them
for weeks before the election took place. They were
warned over and over again that Kelly would carry
to the polls nearly the whole predatory vote of the
city, for the following of the Democratic bosses was
placed at his disposal and he made use of it. There
was but one way to beat him and that was for all
good citizens to vote against him. They did not
do it. .
Having thus planned the campaign and fought the
battle, Kelly is entitled to the spoils. It will be use
less for his allies to come in and attempt to take away
from him the honors or the profits. It is his victory
and not theirs. It will be his convention and not
theirs. It is to him the Republicans of San Fran
cisco must look for a party ticket.
It is but fair to admit that Kelly is the author as
well as the beneficiary of the victory. He planned the
whole campaign from start to finish, and personally
conducted it. It was he who had the primary elec
tion law amended so that he could bring in the State
Central Committee to uphold him in case any attempt
was made to deprive him of control of the party ma
chine in this city. The event has shown how wisely
he planned at. that time, and he deserves credit for
his foresight even when he is condemned for the
unscrupulous means by which his policy was carried
out.
As the head of the victorious ticket in the Twenty
eighth District Kelly will have the public*honors of
the triumph. His name will be the" first called in the
convention. He will have affront seat. He will be
conspicuous in every step in the, proceedings. He is /
not to be the power behind the throne but the power
on the throne, and will be visible to all as the master
of the convention.
BY the primary elections yesterday it is made,
evident that Martin Kelly is the boss of the
voting strength of the Republican party in this
city.- That thousands of Republicans who deem.them
selves good citizens are opposed to him counts for
nothing. Most of such citizens were so indifferent
to politics and to public affairs that they did not take
the trouble to vote. The supporters of Kelly didÂ«
vote, and so it, happens that Kelly shows up this
morning as "the real thing."

THE Great Council of the State of
California. Improved Order of Red
Men, convened yesterday morning
In ' the Post-street wigwam, and
the council fire was kindled by
order of Great Sachem F. O. Brandon
Thera weie present a large number of the
past grand sachems, all the great chiefs
and representatives from all the tribes in
the reservation of California.
Mayor Phelan during the forenoon ap
peared before the Great Council and de
livered an address of welcome to the vis
iting Red Men, and for this he was ten
dered a rising vcte of thanks.
The reports of the great chiefs were pre
sented and referred to the committee on
distribution.
The great sachem in his great sun re
port says:
I am happy to be able to report that ten new
tribes and six councils have been added to our
ranks, all of which are in a prosperous condi
tion, and that the tendency of growth of our
order seems to have nothing- ephemeral about
it, but is onward and upward throughout the
State, the Increase In this reservation durini?
the past Great Sun having reached 956. the
greatest In our history. This result has not
been attained without earnest and persistent
work, and in this connection it is only simple
Justice to say that the greatest credit is due
to the great chief of records. Brother Porter
L. Bliss, who has been indefatigable in his
efforts to place this order upon the pedestal
it is entitled to occupy. No other cares are
permitted to distract his attention from what
he conceives to be his duty to Redmanship, and
our Great Council is to be congratulated upon
having secured the services of a chief who is
courteous and accommodating to the last de
gree, and who seems to devote his whole time,
thought and energies to the work of this or
der.
The fund placed at the disposal of the execu
tive board for the extension of the order has
not lain idly buried in the ground to be brought
forth at the end of the term and returned to
those who gave it. I take pleasure in saying
that the gTeater portion of it has been used in
furtherance of the purpose for which it was
designed, and has contributed in no small de
gree to the happy results achieved in the In
crease of our membership. And if that fund
had been larger it would have been still more
.freely used, for. while much preparatory work
has been done, there are still many important
1 districts in this grrcat State which scarcely
know the name of our order, but where strons
and well-directed efforts, needing the use of
wampum, would establish and place It upon a
firm foundation.

made seventy-s^ven official visitations and
granted many dispensations. He said that
The Great Council of the Degree of
Pocahontas was also in session yesterday
in the wigwam. The representatives of
the several councils; together with the
appointed great chiefs, the following
elected ones: Jennie Weisel. Great Poca
hontas; Mina Rew, Great Wenonah; Kate

During the noon recess yesterday the
great chiefs and representatives were the
guests of the Degree of Pocahontas, who
served them an excellent lunch.
The election for great chiefs will take
place to-day. The only contests will be
for great junior sagamore, in which
Thomas Roche of Bald Eagle Tribe and
C. S. Davis of Mona Tribe will take part
and for representatives to the Great
Council of the United States, three in
number. The candidates are F. D. Bran
don, Henry A. Chase, J. G. Smith and
Colonel A. A. Andrews.

exemplify the work of the adoption de
gree in the competition for the trophy of
fered by the Great Council that it was
discovered the work could not be accom
plished in one night, bo it was decided
that four teams shall work to-night in
Union Square Hall and the others to-mor
row night.
A great deal of time was taken up in
relation to securing a new home for the
order, but at adjournment no conclusion
had been reached.

stedt, grand secretary of the Native Sons,
extending on behalf of the Native Sons of
the Golden West the grasp of true fra
ternal fellowship, greeting the Red Men,
congratulating them on the noble work in
which they are engaged and wishing them
all prosperity, was read.
