Records Bliss it appears that the number
of tribes is sixty-three, ten having been
instituted during the year and one having
surrendered its charter. The total number
of Red Men in the reservation is 5930.

The royal party left the nark at 12:25
p. m. In the first carriage was Queen
Alexandra on the risht and the Empress
on the left. The second carriage contained
King Edward on the right and Emperor
William on the left. Numerous court car
riages bnre away the invited guests and
dignitaries to their homes or to the rail
road, and the crowd rapidly dispersed.
After the body had been lowered Into
the vault the choir from the Berlin
Cathedral sang "Christ Is the Resurrec
tion," by Grell. A prayer followed, and
then the choir sang. "Be Faithful Unto
Death." In the meanwhile the Emperor
stood beside the tomb in a most solemn
manner. There was no weeping, but
King Edward frequently used his hand
kerchief, for beads of perspiration were
coursing his brow. After the brief cere
mony the royal party withdrew, and the
invited guests, military men, diplomats
ard Ftatesmen filed in for the last view
of The coffin.
The coffin was carried into the mauso
leum, followed by the Empress and Em
peror. King Edward and Queen Alexan
dra, the Royal Princes and other mem
bers of royal families, the rest of those
present remaining outside during the last
ceremonies.
Rulers in the Mausoleum.
The procession occupied twenty minutes
in passing. All the bells of Potsdam con
tinued tolling until the procession had
reached the mausoleum and after the ob
sequies were over.
After the military came the highest
clerical dignitaries of Berlin. Including Dr.
Dryander, the court preacher, and Bish
op Aszman, the highest Catholic military
chaplain, in a resplendent purple robe.
Over the head of the coffin were
the Imperial colors, on which was a
crown of state of massive gold and
heavily jeweled. Also on the colors
was a wreath deposited there by
Emperor William, at Cronberg, and two
other wreaths. Behind the coffin marchea
the Emperor, with head erect and display-
Ing the greatest self-possession and dig
nity, but his face was pale and sorrowful,
and he gazed straightforward in a serious
manner. King Edward, who was on the
Emperor's left, walked heavily, and evi
dently found the march fatiguing. Both
Emperor William and King Edward wore
the uniform, of the Second Regiment of
Dragoons. Queen Victoria's regiment.
Embassador White walked with the
Italian Embassador.
Gold Crown on a Coffin.
At the head of the procession rode Ma
jor General von Moltke. nephew of the
famous field marshal. In command of the
military forces, participating in the cere
monies. The procession was in every way
gTand and solemn. The guards du crops,
with blark and white pennants fluttering
at their lance-points, attracted much at
tention, as did the royal page corps, com
posed of cadets from the military acad
emy at Gross Lichterfelde, in bright red
coats, with tight-fitting trousers and
broad hats trimmed with, white lace.
POTSDAM. Aug. 13.-Promptly at 10:30
o'clock the tolling of bells announced the
arrival of the funeral train of the Dow
ager Empress Frederick at Wild Park,
but it was nearly 11 o'clock when the
sound of music In the distance told of the
approach of the funeral procession- Far
down the avenue moving masses ap
peared, now and then halting for the pro
cession to assume Its proper order. As
the procession drew near the notes of the
best-known Juneral marches. played
slowly and plaintively, became audible,
the muffled drums rolling In solemn im
pressiveness.

Empress Frederick's

AUBURN, Aug. 13.â Judge Prewett to
day sentenced Raymond Vogel to-thirty
six years in State prison for highway rob
bery. The Judge also gave him twelve
years for assault with intent to commit
murder. Vogel robbed a boy of 75 cent3,

him he shot fit them, one pistol ball tak

SAN RAFAEL, Aug. 13.â Some miscre
ant snread arsenic on the Belgian hare
farm of H. J. Lentholtz last night and as
a result 150 blue-blooded hares were found
dead this morning, while 100 more are not
likely, to survive. Mr. Lentholtz has 1000
of the animals, all of them of the purest
strain, and the survivors- will have to be
carefully attended to prevent their being
contaminated by the poison. The motive
of the poisoner cannot be surmised, un
less the fact that some of the neighbor-?
of Lentholtz have long complained of the
scent arising from the farm may be taken
as a reason for the act.
Belgian Hares Poisoned.
SAN JOSE, Aug. 13.â Bert Hale, a clerk
employed~by Hart & Roberts, dry goods
men, for the past three years, disappeared
on Saturday night, "and his friends fear
he has committed suicide. He leaves a
wife and two small children, and Mrs.
Hale is unable to account for her hus
band's acts. He owed some bills around
town, and this may account for his dis
appearance. Hale is 26 years of age, and
was married in Alameda three years ago.
He is stout, weighing about 1S5 pounds;
wore a blue sack suit and black derby
hat, and had a small black mustache. A
brother and his father-in-law, Richard
Eustice of Alameda, are here looking for
him. .

A series of petty troubles and minor ac
cidents of various kinds have attended
the building of the celebrated hacienda.
MONTEREY, Aug. 13.â That there is a
"hoodoo" on the Tevls hacienda near this
city is believed more firmly than ever by
the superstitious of this community since
yesterday afternoon, and now the predic
tion ir. that the house will never be com
pletely finished. Foreman Whitby and
George Cooper, a skilled artisan, were on
top of a high scaffolding adjusting a part
of the carved wood ceiling decoration in
the billiard room. The scaffolding sudden
ly gave way and both men fell to the
floor, sustaining severe injuries. Whitby
struck on his side on top of a post and
was badly hurt internally, but it is be
lieved he will recover. Cooper was not so
badly Injured and will probably be able to
resume work in a few days.
Accident in the New Man
sion.
Two Men Badly Injured in the Latest
HOODOO REIGNS OVER
THE TEVIS HACIENDA
The Ringling Bros, are justly proud cf
this item in their list of circus values. In
fact, the menagerie part of the Ringling
Bros, show is rated above all others for
its remarkably rare collection of wild ani
mals. Only recently a giraffe was added.
This is the only one known to be In cap
tivity and cost a small fortune. Giraffes
are fast disappearing, and to find one of
late years seemed impossible. The giraffe
was captured In February .last and has
been journeying to this country by slow
stages since. .The zoological collection of
the circus was further benefited last week
by the arrival of two baby li^r.s and a
baby yak. There are over 500 horses with
the Ringling circus, more than 200 of
which" are '.performing* animals of fine
breeding and high-strung natures. The
wonderful way in which . eighty-one of
these thoroughbreds respond to the com
mand of John O'Brien, without 1 whip, bit
or bridle, stay or strap of any kind, is one
of the sensational acts in a list of one
hundred or more in the circus programme.
The trained elephants, numbering thirty
in all, with the Ringling Bros.' circus, to
be here week after next, are a remarkable
feature. These great, cumbersome ani
mals are put through a course of tricks
truly amazing. They play musical instru
ments, sing coarpely, dance heavily, stand
on their heads,- form pyramids, smartly
waltz and actually go through the humor
ous Incidents of a farcical skit.
Go Through a Series of Very
Amazing Tricks.
The Cumberson Animals Trained to

The grand ball In the Mechanics' Pa
vilion in the evening Was a most success
ful affair. More than 6500 ladies and gen
tlemen filled the large building and the
many colored toilets of the ladies as they

To-day there will be the election for
great chiefs. Mlna Rew will be advanced
to great Pocahontas. but for the other
elective offices, except for great keeper of
records and great keeper of wampum,
there will probably be contests.
On Thursday night teams from Poca
hontas, Alfarata. Yosemlte and Ramona
councils will exemplify the adoption de
gree in a contest for a $75 silk flag.
During the afternoon the council was
visited by Porter L. Bliss, Thomas Roche
and V. F. Northrup, a committee from
the Red Men, who each gave a short talk
for the good of the order.
The great keeper of records in her re
port showed that the membership of the
order is 1735, that there was a gain of 319
members, ' that there are 'twenty-four
councils in the order and that two sur
rendered their charters. These and other
reports were referred.
After the routine of opening the council
In the prescribed form and the appoint
ment of Carrie L. Johnson, Hattle Whlp
ple and Edith Fish as a press committee,
Jennie Weisel, the great Pocahontas. pre
sented her annual report, in which; she
gave an account of the work done and
congratulated the order on its advance
ment and the fact that it had made the
greatest gain in membership it ever made.
The only recommendation she made was
that a State organizer should be ap
pointed.

PORTLAND, Or., Aug. 13.â JessÂ© D.
Carr's fence, which Incloses 80,000 acres
of Government land In Southern Oregon
and Northern California, must come
down. Judge Gilbert, in the United States
Circuit Court, to-day approved the $10,000
bond of the Jesse D. Carr Land and Live
stock Company, on appeal from . Judge
Bellinger's decision to the United States
Circuit Court of Appeals, but refused to
grant a stay of proceedings. The bond
was signed by John P. Irish of San Fran
cisco and James D. Carr, and at the foot
was a statement signed by Judge Morrow
to the effect that he considered the sure
ties sufficient. The bond was sent here
for the approval of Judge Gilbert and for
him to decide whether a stay of proceed
ings should be granted under it or not.
The matter came up before Judge Gilbert
this morning. No argument was made, as
none was necessary. Judge Hunt ap
proved the bond and indorsed on it, "No
stay of proceedings in this case is al
lowed." Mr. Hall at once took the fol

creasing,' and the elevator concerns, both In
the Northwest and ; Southwest, were selling.
The Corn crowd were free" sellers of Wheat.

NEW YORK. Aug. 13.â Business to-day was
on a small scale, but there was a wider distri
bution of the dealings. Strength was shown
by many Important stocks, but the general
movement continued Irregular. The opening
was higher on a resumption of yesterday's
strength and the good tone of the London mar
ket. The fortnightly settlement abroad was
arranged satisfactorily and arbitrage houses
bought more liberally here than for some time.
First prices were not leng maintained, as trad
ers realized, but when a demand arose for the
Eastern trunk lines and bituminous coal road
etocks the entire market rallied sharply. Sugar
was about the only notable exception to the
day's strength, the bears lowering it 5 points
at one time and selling steadily when it ral
lied. There was little news of importance bear
ing en either the crop conditions or the steel
strike and the influence was less marked.
Frequent relapses In prices were made causing
the Pacific stocks to sag temporarily under yes
ttrday's close. Prices rallied easier, and as
there were large purchases of St. Paul and
the Eastern stocks shorts covered more gen
erally- in the market. The improvement in the
trunk lines and solt coal stocks was partly due
to the benefits' expected to accrue from the
recent formation of a bituminous coal com
bination. New York Central made the largest
rise of 3V t . but Baltimore and Ohio, Pennsyl
vania, Chesapeake and Ohio and Norfolk and
Western averaged 2 points better. St. Paul
was taken in good amounts and advanced 3%
on reports that there might be an increased
dividend declared next month. Short buying
was considered to be responsible for the im
proved tone of the market, but the waning in
fluence of the crop conditions and the steel
strike was also considered. Western and Pa
cific stocks followed pretty closely In the wake
cf St. Paul on its advance and the grain-car
rying roads were helped by the heaviness of
the grain market. Little interest was mani
fested generally In the United States Steel
Etocks and they moved quite generally inde
pendent of other stocks. An early risÂ» of %
and % respectively in the common and pre
ferred were followed by a gradual decline and
they ended only % and hi higher. The large
payments made by the sub-treasury on account
of army and navy credits and some receipts
of gold at Pacific Coast points caused a loss
of about $1,500,000 by the banks to the sub
treasury up to yesterday to be turned into a
sain of about as much. The stock market
closed strong and at about the best for the
principal features.
The bond market was quiet and firm. Total
sales, par value, $360,000.
United States bends were all unchanged on
the last call.
Nezv York Stock Market.

DECIDUOUS FRUITS,
Supplies of Grapes continue moderate, and
while the demand is not very active, prices
are .steady. Â¦ .
Apricots are almost gone, and Figs are hardly
seen, except in a scattering way. Nectarines
continue dull.
Lemons are easy at the decline already noted
and the market Is better supplied than for some
time. Oranges and Limes are firmly held.
There is no change worthy of note in Ber
ries.
While there is plenty cf fruit lying around,
there is a scarcity of real sound peaches. Pears
and Plums,, and these fruits rule steady In
consequence. Overripe fruit has to go for what
it will bring. There are plenty of melons of
all kinds here, more than can be worked off
on the wharf.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
CHICAGO, Aug. 13.â Lower, cables in face of
the strength exhibited : here yesterday rather
upsft the calculations of the â¢ bulls in the
wheat pit to-day and the result was that the
market was somewhat nervous. Fluctuations
during the day were within a narrow limit and
there were no features of interest in the trade.
September opened %@%c lower at 73%@73%c,
and on covering by shorts the price advanced
to 73%c, but declined again to 73@73%c on sell
ing by local longs. The close was . a shade
lower at 73%@73%c.
The conflicting reports as to the relative
amount of damage done the corn crop by the
recent drought were reflected somewhat in the
attitude taken by local bulls, who were in
clined to get rid of 'their holdings. Trade,
however, was very small. September closed
nrm and 34c lower at 59%@59%c.
Oats opened lower in sympathy with other
grains. Trading was within a narrow range.
Prices . were firm all - day. September closed
with a rain of %c at 36%c.
Provisions opened steady and trading was
moderately active. The principal feature was
good buying of ribs by one of the leading pack-
Ing houses. Selling was scattered. September
pork closed. 12%c higher, lard a shade higher
and ribs 10c higher..

OAKLAND, Aug. 13.â The death 'of
Henry. Maddern, a pioneer of this city,
occurred last night at his residence, 16G7
Broadway, where he had been seriously
ill for ten days from a complication of
troubles. Mr. Maddern had been in the
plumbing business for thirty years. - He
was prominent in fraternal affairs, being
affiliated with the Masons, Order of East
ern Star, Odd Fellows, :Rebekah Degree
Knights of Pythias and the Rathbone Sis
ters. Mr. Maddern leaves six childrenâ
Henry T., William A., Minnie S., Robert
E. and Flora E. Maddern and Mrs. Jack
London. The deceased was an uncle of
Mrs. Minnie Maddern-Fiske, the actress
He.was a native of Cornwall, England 67
years of age. "The funeral will' be held
Wednesday afternoon at 2:30 o'clock from
the family residence.
Death, of Henry Maddern.
"Washington retains in the 1900 census
the distinction which is had in the 1S90
census of having more colored Inhabitants
than any other city of the United States.
The explanation of "Washington's popu
larity as a place of residence for colored
people is. that it is the winter quarters of
many thousand negroes who at the begin
ning of spring: begin their exodus to
Northern watering places and to Virginia
and Maryland resorts. The cost of living
for colored people in "Washington is not
high. The climate Is equable, the national
Government does not authorize restric
tions against l colored people such as pre
vail - In most . Southern States as to resi
dence and means of transportation. There
is no discrimination against them in the
"Washington courts and the large transi
ent-population of the District of Colum
bia during the sessions of Congress and
when the public departments are under
full; headway causes a large demand for
colored employes.â Chicago Journal.

Odd Funeral Customs.
A" correspondent tells this story about
Carters Grove, an old manor house near
Yorktown, Va.: "It is one of the customs
of Carters Grove, as old as the mansion
itself, to have a bowl of mint julep al
ways standing upon the sideboard, and
people from Willlamsburg or Yorktown
and the neighboring planters who passed
that way never failed to stop for refresh
ment. Dr. Booth, the present owner, says
of one of the former proprietors, who had
the gout so badly that he could not walk
and used to lie all day long on a couch in
the corner of the dining-room and growl
about the pain he suffered: 'He had a
Shetland pony, trained to lie down on the
floor beside him. When he felt thirsty, he
climbed upon -the pony's back, rode over
to the punch bowl, drank freely of the
beverage that always could be found
there and then returned to his corner and
rolled upon his couch, while the patient
pony would lie down beside him until his
services were needed again.' "âRecord-
Herald.
Customs of Carters Grove, Va.

OAKLAND, Aug. 13.-City Attorney
Johnson this afternoon decided that the
appointment of Joseph Courtney, brother
of the Councilman, as street sprinkling in
spector was illegal. ; ' â¢
The City Attorney finds that for several
reasons the appointment is faulty. First,
the Coupcil created the. position by reso
lution Instead of by ordinance, which
alone makes the position illegal.- In the
second place, the appointment was made
by the Council, though the charter gives
the Board of Public "Works control of the
streets of the city. Under .this provision
of the charter it would be necessary to
make the position so that it could be filled
by the Board of Public : Works.

i by Resolution and Could Not

Mrs. Scott is an elderly woman, with
but little experience in this world's af
fairs. She says McCoy induced her to go
into a great scheme for making money.
He was to take up Government land at
Bakersfield and a fortune was to be won
by reselling the grants at a great advance
over the cost of procuring the land. The
proposition teemed fair and Mrs. Scott
was delighted when the first transaction
came to a head a few days ago and a pur
chaser was found for a piece of the land.
So pleased was the woman that she did
not hesitate a moment to cash a check
signed by "J. A. Thompson," the ostensi
ole purchaser. But when the Thompson
check was sent to the bank and it had
gone through the trade channels the al
leged forgery was quickly uncovered.
Mrs. Scott waited In vain for a settle
ment with her partner. The adjustment
was not forthcoming. After waiting what
she termed a reasonable length of time
Mrs. Scott decided upon a prosecution
Yesterday she located McCoy at Sacra
mento, and the aggrieved woman has
tened there. She swore to a warrant
charging McCoy with forgery. He was
arrested, his bail being set at $1000.

The City Council was determined to dic
tate who should be the attorney, but it
split over the "who." Some wanted W.
W. Foote and others wanted William R.
Davis, and between the two City Attorney
Johnson announced that he wanted the
election of his assistant If one was to be
allowed him. The original proposition
was to set aside "between $6000 and $8000
for the appeal.
The matter was settled, as far as the
Council was concerned, last night, when
ft abandoned all effort to name either W.
W. Fcote or William R. Davis, and it
passed the whole matter up to City Attor
ney Johnson, leaving him to get out of it
as best he can and take the responsibility
of success or failure. The result was the
ordinance that was passed last night,
which passes everything up to City At
torney Johnson j most, effectually.
It Is understood that George M.- Shaw
will be selected- to -assist Mr. Johnson,
though the City Attorney declines to dis
cuss the matter until the ordinance be
comes a law.
After several months of delay the City
Council has made some provision for the
appeal of the suit brought by the Contra
Costa Water Company against the city of
Oakland over water rates, in which Judge
Hart delivered his remarkable decision
that has resulted in the doubling of the
water rates of the city. It- is true that
the arrangements for the appeal are not
such as would be deeme'd the most liberal,
but they are regarded as the best that can
.be secured from the solid seven in the.
present municipal legislature.
The Council has passed to print an ordi
nance which creates the position of Dep
uty City Attorney, the duties of that offi
cial being to assist in the preparation of
the appeal in the water case. The salary
is fixed at $200 a month and appointment
is to be made by City Attorney Johnson.
The term of the deputy is to be during the
pleasure of the City Attorney.
This ordinance was the result of the at
titude of City Attorney Johnson. The
City Attorney and the City Council have
not been in that sweet accord and happy
harmony that should characterize
branches of the municipal government.
City Attorney Johnson openly announced
that if any one was to be retained to
carry on the water suit litigation he
would like to have Robert Y. Hayr,e. the
man who conducted the suit in the Supe
rior Court. But the City Council dis
missed Judge Hayne without benefit of
clergy, and then began a contest as to
who should assist Johnson.

and it is expected that all of those who
have opposed the ordinance will support

License Inspector T. J. Newell to-day
made a demand upon Walter Meese . for
his license. He was met with the state
ment that he would pay any license tax
upon signs that were beyond the line of
the building, but that he would not pay

a specific charge for signs attached to
buildings, License Inspector Newell could
not make the exception. He applied for a
warrant for the arrest of Mr. Meese and
the warrant was served this afternoon."

ALAMEDA. Aug. 13.â The Oakland
Transit Company announces that It will
abandon entirely the Alameda terminus of
the Leona Heights electric road. This is
one result of the absorrtion by the com
pany of the Haywards line. The Leona
Heights road ever since it was built has
b*>Â«?n making connections with the Ala
meda cars at the tidal canal bridge at
Park street. At that point passenger*
could take cars for any part of Oakland
or Alameda. Xow the passengers will
transfer at the point on the county road
in Fruitvale where the road crosses the
Haywards line, the latter -being the means
to reach Oakland. Alameda people will
be compelled to transfer twice under the
new system, first to the Haywards line
find then to the Alameda line. People
who formerly took the main trains direct
to the city via Bather station will now
have to transfer to the station at Twenty
third avenue.

Haywards Road.

The placing of '05 on the hills Is a chal
lenge to the sophomores, and shows that
the freshmen desire a more intimate ac
quaintance with members of the class
above them. ' \
During the rush troubles of last year a
committee of senior boys was placed in
charge of affairs and acted as a court of
arbitration and for the suppression of
class spirit. President Wheeler urged the
necessity of the one class ruling, but it
took six months for the peace plans to
achieve the desired results. Collegians
are now speculating whether the new
class - will require a term's tussling and
another volume of faculty edicts against
conflicts with upper class men to calm
the intrants, or whether they will slip
quietly on under the rule of perpetual
peace and. do nothing. The juniors, at
once the friends "and enemies of the fresh
menâthe protectors in case of strife and
the instigators to conflict in case of peace
â are still musing over their freshly paint
ed plugs and the prominence In college
life another year has brought them, and
are not alive to the fact that the campus
is covered with young hopefuls in need of
experience. When the freshmen are all
assembled Monday for registration the
juniors will undoubtedly remember.
BERKELEY, Aug. 13.â A crowd of spir
ited freshmen, not yet posted on univer
sity rules and regulations, placed the class
number on the hills back of the college
buildings last night. This morning '05
stood out on the brown hills in fifty-foot
letters of paper and lime, much to the ad
miration of the Intrants. The sophomores,
still remembering the experiences of their
bumptious days of a year ago, have ig
nored the figures thus far, but pent up
class patriotism has a limit of elasticity,
and the numbers are not expected to re
main long on the hills.

OAKLAND, Aug. 13.â John Relschman
and V. Ivancovich, young men about 20
years old, were arrested to-day by De
tectives Holland and Shorey for the theft
of money and valuable jewelry belonging
to Carl Schilling, a son, of A. Schilling,
the San Francisco importer. The stealing
was done at the Piedmont baths, where
young Schilling had placed his clothing in
a locker before going to the swimming
tank. The key to the locker was hidden
in Schilling's shoes, which he wore to the
tank, leaving them in his disrobing room.
The thieves stole the key and presenting
it at the cashier's desk were given Schill
ing's property. The stolen articles con
sisted of a diamond ring, a diamond stud
ded university pin, a gold watch and
about $10 in coin.

A FESTIVAL of Irish music, song
and speech will be held at the
Metropolitan Temple to-morrow
evening under the auspices- of the %
Gaelic League of California. The
A special feature of the entertainment
will be the singing of a few Gaelic songs,
with harp accompaniment, by Mme. Ca
rusi of the Tivoli Opera Company. Mme.
Carusi has been the principal attraction
at all the great Eastern Irish celebrations.
In addition to the foregoing numbers,
Miss Annie Rooney, Mrs. John Maddln,
David Manlloyd. . S. J. Sandy, Jeremiah
Deasy and W. A. Sabin will take part in
the concert. Miss Nora Sandy will be the
piano accompanist.
The Rev. Peter C. Yorke will deliver an
address in Gaelic and the Rev. James Mc-
Donald will deliver an English speech.
Gaelic songs will be rendered by the Gae
lic Choral Society under the direction of
Mr. Sandy. Instrumental music will be
rendered by the Gaelic School Orchestra.
programme arranged for the occasion I;
a splendid one.
MODESTO, Aug. 13.â Virgil Upton and
William Thornburg were severely burned
Jn the Coast Range Mountains on Sunday.
The- young men went camping and their
campflre got beyond their control and set
fire to the hay which was under their
wagon. The wagon, two shot
guns and a watch were burned and both
young men sustained severe burns about
the hands, feet and face in saving the
horses. They were compelled to ride
twelve miles for medical attendance. Aft
er getting beyond control the fire extended
to the brush and dry feed in the canyon
back of Grayson and a mountain fire Is
still raging.

MacRae arrested Brown and two com
panions, Charles Kelly and Max Jacobs,
late Sunday night, September 23, 1900. on
Buena Vista avenue for fast driving. The
officer overhauled the young men by
boarding a passing street car after they
had disregarded his command to stop.
After he had stopped their rig he told
Brown to get out, and handcuffed him.
MacRae then had Brown get back Into
the buggy and was getting In himself to
drive it to police headquarters when the
horse was started, throwing him between
the wheels. He ordered Brown, who held
the reins, to stop, threatening to shoot
if his command were not heeded. Tho
horse kept on and the policeman fired
three shots at his escaping prisoners. He
then boarded another car and arrived at
the Webster-street bridge before they did.
He found that Brown was badly wounded
by his fire. At the Receiving Hospital the
surgeons gave it as their opinion that thei
injury was a mortal one, and the wounded
man died two days later. MacRae was
arrested and charged with manslaughter.
Considerable difficulty was experienced
in finding Jurors who thought a policeman
justified in shooting a man attempting to
escape after arrest for a misdemeanor
simply. Attorney A. A. Moore, who with
Emil Nusbaumer appeared for the de
fense, insisted that every jnror accepted
should admit the duty of an officer to
hold every prisoner at whatever cost.
.' MacRae since his arrest has been out
on bail performing 1 his customary duties
en the Alameda police force.

Brown In Alameda last September was
begun before Judge Greene to-day.

Special, Dispatch to The Call.
, ?, r V c Â£ arIes von Radesky is firm in the
belief that \\ anner'e death was brought
about by natural causes, and he scouts
the idea that strangulation or brutal
treatment had any part in it. He savs
that half the statements attributed to
him in the effort to support a story with
out foundation axe false.
"You know," said the doctor, "that
after death any wound or bruise that
might have been received In life is very
greatly magnified. It usually looks ten
times worse than It really Is. So It was
in the case of Wanner. The blackened
?-ye looked bad, but it was not enough to
kill him. The other discolorations were
not bruises, but blood marks. As for the
fractured fkull and the broken jaw that
is all buncombe. The man died from nat
ural causes."
The body is resting in a receiving vault
and no investigation is likely.
"Thf ro was really only one mark of vio
lence." said Gallagher, "and that was the
blackened left eye. I understand that that
was received before he left here. The oth
er discolorations were caused by coagu
lated blood. Wanner was a large and
full-blooded man. and unless a body such
as his is taken charge of immediately
after death coapulation Invariably results.
If the head is below the level of the rest
of the body the blood all goes to the head.
In this case the body was not treated un
til three days after death.
"The marks on the neck and chin which
some of Wanner? friends thought were
bruises were simply the marks made by
the instrument we use to keep the jaw's
together until rigor mortis sets in The
statement that there Is evidence of a
broken jaw is absolutely without founda
tion, as I know positively there was noth
ing of the kind. I am satisfied that Wan
ner's death was due to natural causes."
"I saw the body." said George Odell
Gallagher's assistant, "and I saw the dis
colorations. but I am positive that they
were caused by coagulated blood. The
idea that they were bruises resulting
from rough handling- never suggested it
self to me. All the statements attributed
to me regarding these so-called bruises
are untrue."
Wanner was a partner in the Tivoli sa
loon, on Park street, and drank excessive
ly before he lost his mind, which made
his ca.se the more complicated. Superin
tendent Smith said that Wanner had to
be strapped down all the time, as he was
f-o hard to handle. The body arrived in
Alameda Sunday tnornini?. tv.o days after
death. It was taken ctnrm of and pre
pared for burial by the Gallagher Under
taking Ccmpanv. Hugh Gallagher, who
had charge of the funeral preparations,
says that there? were no evidences of
brutal treatment on the body.
The statement of Superintendent Smith
of the Ukiah asylum regarding Wanner's
death is that he died from acute mania.
. ALAMEDA, Aug. IS.â The story that
Louis "Wanner met a violent death at the
hands of attendants at the Ukiah State
Insane Aeylum is the offspring of an idle
statement. Wanner's death was due to a
natural cause, the superintending physi
cian of the asylum ascribing it to "ma
r.ia." His skull was not fractured, his
jaw was not broken and there are no
marks of violence on his body except a
discoloration of the left eye caused by his
own frantic efforts at the Receiving Hos
pital. What Wanner's friends took for
evidences of brutal treatment are nothing
more than the discolorations on the body
caused by coagulated blood.
George Foster, a friend of the deceased,
innocently stalled the story of foul play
when he made the -casual statement to a
group of friends that Wanner's body was
badJy bruised and suggested that he
might have been killed at the asylum.
The news spread, and many of Wanner's
friends came lo view the remains. They
were deceived l.y the facial discolorations
and went away with the impression that
the man had been murdered.
Wanner was violent when he was taken
from Alarneda to the Oakland Receiving
Hospital for examination for insanity two
weeks ago. It took two men to hold him
down until the hospital was reached. At
the hospital it was necessary to strap him
to a cot, so violent were his actions. When
the time came to take him to Ukiah he
became stil! more frantic and attacked
Warden Paee. The men had a desperate
fight, out of which the Warden came vic
torious after a terrific struggle. It was
then that Wanner received the blackened
eye which marks him even in death.

SAN FRANCISCO MATRON
WHO IS FAMOUS IN PARIS
Before leaving here for abroad Mrs. Dor
sey had done much excellent work on
both china and ivory, and her intention
was to perfect herself in the latter art un
der the best masters of the Continent.
She did not intend to take up ivory por
traiture: as a profession, but merely to
perfect herself In a refined and delightful
accomplishment. Her success, however,
has been eo phenomenal -that sho now
finds her diversion a very profitable
source of income, as she has risen to the
highest rank among artists in her line of
work, and the least valuable of her paint
ings bring from $500 upward. All of her
work is eagerly sought for, and she has
been given the very highest praise by such
a severe critic as Jules Michel.

' r ADVISORY BOARD ORGANIZED.
At a meeting of shareholders at this office
Saturday, August 10, an Advisory Board of the
Montezuma Mining Company was organized for
California and other shareholders of the south-
west. The beard consists of nine members,
whose names will be later announced by cir-
cular. Thirty-five shareholders were present at
the meeting and stock to the number of about
400.CC0 shares was represented. Mr. J. S. C.
Thompson was elected delegate to the annual
stockholders' meeting in Tacbma, September
A DIVIDEND DECLARED.
The Montezuma Mining Company has an-
nounced Its first quarterly dividend, payable
October 31, 1001, of $1 per thousand shares of
paid up stock; that being equivalent to 5 per
cent | per annum on the average cost of shares
for the past six months. This dividend comes
from a single sale of coal and agrees with the
policy to which tlfe company is committed.
Other and larger dividends may be confidently
expected from the same source as the produc-
ing capacity of the coal-mines Is Increased.
Present price of shares Is ISc cash or 21c on
Installments. These are strictly . ground-floor
prices and the expenditure of a little money
will put one In possession of a considerable
block of what is confessedly , the best mining
stock on this or any .ether market at the price!
He who waits for the low-priced stock of a
new company will lose the three years that the
Montezuma has gained from its success in
placing of shares. Write to* us for full infor-
mation concerning this : big, strong, safe min-
ing company of Washington.

DENVER, Aug. 13.â The Supreme Court
to-day granted a writ of supersedeas set
ting aside the decision of District Judge
Dixon, which declared the new revenue
law invalid. The supersedeas was granted
on the formal request of the Attorney
General's office and was obtained for the
purpose of preventing any possible inter
ference with the work of the State Board
of Assessors by Judge Dixon.

OF INTEREST TO PEOPLE
.OF THE PACIFIC COAST
The resolutions adopted declare that thÂ©
Japanese were examined in a harsh man
ner after the white cabin passengers had
been passed without any examination at
all, and the removal of Dr. Cofer is asked
for. It was declared by the mass meeting
that the manner of examination was un
necessary, unusual and in violation of
common decency and an insult to woman
kind at large, regardless of race or color.
The Japanese declared that it was an un
just and unreasonable discrimination be
tween the Caucasian and Oriental passen
gers and a direct and personal insult to
the Japanese Government In the person of
its Vice Consul to Honolulu.
The passengers . over whose treatment
the complaints were made are Vice Con
sul S. Okabe and his wife, who arrived
here on July 25 on the steamship America
Maru. When the vessel arrived a case of
sickness, suspected to be plague, was re
ported on board, and the vessel was kept
in quarantine until the nature of the sick
ness was investigated. It was found that
the man was not suffering from any dan
gerous disease and the vessel was re
leased from Quarantine. Okabe anji his
wife, who were cabin passengers, were
subjected to a physical examination under
what it is -claimed were deeply humiliat
ing circumstances, while the other cabin
passengers were passed without such ex
aminations,'and there was great indigna
tion among Japanese here when the mat
ter was reported ashore.
HONOLULU. H. T., Aug. 6.â A large
mass meeting of. Japanese residents of.
Honolulu was held last Friday evening to
protest against the actions of Dr. L. E..
Cofer, United States quarantine officer
for this port, in subjecting -a Japanese
consular officer and his wife to physical
examination when they arrived here on
the 25th of July. The local Japanese Con
sul, MIkl Salto, took 'part in the meeting
and feeling ran high, ending in the adop
tion of very severe resolutions, copies of
which will be sent to the Japanese Minis
ter at Washington and to President Mc-
Klnley and other officials at the national
capital. i
Charges Are Made That He
Insulted a Vice Con-'
sul's Wife.

GEORGE R. FLETCHER, Manager.THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, AUGUST 14, 1901.
The Scott Anniversary.
St. Andrew's Society will celebrate the
one hundred and thirtieth anniversary of
the birth of Sir Walter Scott Friday
night in Scottish Hall. An excellent liter
ary programme will be presented, to be
followed by a social dance.
The annual meeting of the North Pa
cific Coast Railroad was held on Monday
last. After the regular routine business
had been gone through the election of
officers to serve for the ensuing year was
held and resulted in the following gentle
men being chosen: Board of directorsâ J.
B. Stetson, C. de Guigne, J. C. Coleman,
C. R. Winslow, Captain A. H. Payson. J.
H. Meyer and A. H. Small; J. B. Stetson,
president; J. C. Coleman, vice president;
J. H. Meyer, treasurer, and F. B. Latham,
secretary.

Thomas' Corbett, a laborer, was arrested
yesterday on a charge of mayhem. Cor
bett boarded at the lodging-house of Mrs.
Phillipine Aymard, 403 Broadway, and
was forced to leave because he could not
pay $45 due for board and" lodging. He
went there yesterday under the influence
of liquor, and when Mrs. Aymard told
him to learve it Is claimed he grabbed hold
of her and bit her on the shoulder and
finger. In trying to escape Corbett fell
downstairs and was wounded on the fore
head.*.
Accused of Mayhem.
At the approach of the officers the boys
left their plunder and started to escape.
One of them succeeded, but the other,
whose name is John Welch, was over
taken at the corner of Pine and Kearny
streets after a spirited chase. The boy
who escaped is known to the police andi
he will undoubtedly be placed under ar
rest to-day.
Herbold at the time of the hold-up waa
on his way home. He lost nothing in the
affray, but the boys, in attempting to
extract his watch from his pocket, broke
the chain.
The pair of juvenile footpads met Her
bold in a saloon at the corner of East and
Mission streets, where he had been im
bibing. The boys followed him until he
reached a secluded spot at the corner of
Commercial and . Sansome streets and
there attacked him. Before rifling him
of his property they beat Herbold severe
ly about the head and cut a deep gash
over his right eye. They did not succeed
in rendering him unconscious, and when
they attempted to remove his watch and
chain he cried out for help. His cries at
tracted the attention of Special Officers
Gillen and Whelan and Policeman Du
Guire, who immediately ran to his as
sistance.
Two newsboys, .neither ' of whom Is
more than 16 years of age, attempted a
daring robbery last night about 10 o'clock
at the corner of Commercial and San
some streets. Only the arrival of two
special officers and a policeman saved
their victim, William Herbold, a cook on
the transport Mea-ie, from the loss of his
watch and money.

borhood and finally learned that the thief

Wanted for Forgery.
SAN JOSE. Aug. 13.â S. P. Bernstein,
who is said to be the son of a wealthy
Los Angeles family, is wanted here on a
charge of forgery preferred by E. S.
Blake of Fresno. Blake cashed. a check
for Bernstein, drawn on the Leropian
brothers of Fresno, and it is pronounced
worthless. Bernstein was formerly con
nected with a theatrical troupe.
. LEWISTON, Idaho, Aug. 13.-Five' pris
oners escaped from the County Jail last
night by cutting a, hole eighteen finches
square through a thick brick wall E lined
with steel. They used a brace and bit and
were assisted by accomplices outside the
jail. The fugitives are: John R. Reamer,
Frank Smith and Charles Smith, catttle
thieves; Charles Paine,- burglar, and Max
Mox, an Indian. No trace of them has
been found.
Five Prisoners Escape.'
A conference of the teachers of the
eighth grades in the public schools was
held yesterday afternoon in the assembly
room of the Board of Education. Direc
tors Kincaid, Mark and Denman were
present to hear suggestions, from the
te&chers regarding the conduct of their
claf-&es. Some teachers expressed the opin
ion that the studies as laid down In the
course were a little too comprehensive and
It required much hard work to finish them
within the prescribed term. Teachers who
have two grades in their classes pleaded
for the study of only one poem instead of
two, as difficulty was encountered in com
pleting the study since the "no home
study law!' has been put in force. . The
board decided to take the suggestions un
der consideration, and it is quite likely
that some plan will be adopted whereby
the course of study will be modified in
some unimportant particulars.
Eighth Grade Teachers Confer.
Winter Zero Swartsel, an enthusiastic
cyclist of Dayton, Ohio, arrived here yes
terday, having come the entire distance
awheel. He is visiting his brother, S. J.
Swartsel, who " lives at 1016 Broderick
street. Swartsel left his Ohio home eight
weeks ago in company with S; A. Popst,
an Otterbein student." Popst acquired cold
feet in Southwest Missouri and severed
his connection with Winter Zero, who ac
complished the journey alone. The trip
was uneventful, the most exciting ' inci
dent occurring on the plains, when W. Z.
paced a bull for a half-mile spurt. The
distance ridden, as recorded on his cy
clcmeter, was 32S8 miles and never a punc
ture during the whole journey. Swartsel
rode from Dayton to New York' last sum
mer in company with his brother Horace.
He says he prefers the Rocky Mountains
as a bicycle path to the Ozarks in L Mis
souri. Putting his last summer's trip with
the one just finished, he has wheeled from
the Atlantic to the Pacific. He will return
by rail... . : ' - :

Joseph Knowlton, charged with having
embezzled S120 from the Ira H. and J. H.
Doolittle Hotel Company, has applied for
a writ of prohibition restraining Police
Judge Mogan from proceeding with the
hearing of the charge. Knowlton alleges
that the local Police Court has" no Juris
diction and that probable cause for his
detention does not exist. t
Knowlton Denies Charge.
The Oceanic Steamship Company's So
noma also made port last night. She
came in through the fog about 9 p. m. and
anchored In the lower bay. She also was
late in getting to Honolulu and did not
get away until considerably after her
schedule time on the 6th Inst. She will
dock this morning and land her passen
gers as soon as she passes quarantine.
People , at Honolulu . were wondering
what was the matter with the Peking, as
they did not know she was over thirty
hours late leaving Yokohama. 'Then she
encountered heavy weather on the run
across and that delayed her still more.
She reached Honolulu at 3 a. m. on the
5th and sailed again the next day.
The gunboat Bennington from the Or
ient and the cruiser Philadelphia from
Samoa were at Honolulu when the Peking
called. The Philadelphia was to sail on
the 7th inst. The gunboat Petrel from the
Orient left Honolulu for San Francisco on
the 3d Inst.

The Pacific Mall Company's steamship
City of Peking arrived from the Orient
via Honolulu yesterday. She sailed from
the islands on the 6th inst.. three days
late, and made a fair run to San Fran
cisco. She brought up 47 cabin and 20 sec
ond cabin passengers and 133 Chinese in
the steerage.
During the two years In which Dr. Hut
slnpiller officiated as minister of the
church he now leaves a debt of $10,500 was
paid off, leaving the church free of debt.
An increase of $1000 a. year in his salary,
with the almost positive assurance of a
permanent pastorate in his new field was
one of the principal reasons why he left
San Francisco.
While the members of his congregation
are loth to lose Dr. Hutsinpiller, they
realize that he is going to a church where
his ministerial labors will be as fully ap
preciated as they were in this city. A
farewell reception will be arranged for the
departing clergyman, and he will be ac
companied by the good wishes of his flock
in his newly chosen field.
Rev. Dr. Hutslnpiller was formerly pas
tor of a. church in Athens, Ohio, and he
filled many pulpits In that State. He left
a pastorate in Erie, Pa., to accept the call
to me Central Methodist Episcopal
Church * in this city. The minister Is a
graduate of the Ohio Wesleyan, Univer
sity and is about 48 years of age. â¢ Before
coming here he had been traveling in
Europe for a period of twelve' months. On
his return to this country he received
many calls from Eastern churches, but
believing there fw a pood field on. this
coast he accepted the call from the Cen
tral M. E. Church. At one time he was
pastor- of a Congregational church in
London.
Only two -months ago Dr. Hutsinpiller
was offered the presidency of the Ohio
State University of Athens, Ohio, at a.
munificent salary, but he declined the of
fer, preferring to remain with his flock
in this city. The minister will proceed to
the Methodist Conference, which is to be
held in Chatfield. Minn., on September
25. This* conference will be presided over
by the T^ght Rev. Bishop Cranston, the
resident Bishop of the Northwest, and Dr.
Hutsinpiller will undoubtedly be assigned
to the church which has made an urgent
call for his services.
Rev. Dr. S. D. Hutsinpiller, pastor of
the Central Methodist Church, located on
Mission street, notified Presiding Elder
W. W. Case of the California diocese
yesterday that he had decided to resign
the pastorate of the church. - Dr. Hutsin
piller resigns to accept a call from the
Hennepin-avenue Methodist Episcopal
Church of Minneapolis, Minn.

TWO ACRES OF STONE
ABE BEADY FOB, BLASTING
charge of Thirty Tons of High
Grade Explosives.
Top of Arch Bock A-waits the Dis-
There will be a mighty explosion at
noon to-morrow. Two acres of the sur
face of Arch Rock in the bay will be sent
flying skyward by the propulsive energy
of thirty tons of high grade powder, all
of which will go off at once. Contractor
Axman. who has the task of removing
the rocks frcra the harbor of San Fran
cisco, says that this will be the largest
explosion that has ever taken place in
the United States, with one exception.
When the greatest of all the rocks in
Hell Gate, in New York harbor, was
blasted away several years ago the ex
plosives were little more In quantity. But
when the top of Arch Rock goes shooting
into space the explosive employed will be
nearly four times greater than that used
â¢when Shag Rock was blasted. The dan
ger line for vessels during the explosion
has been placed at a distance of one mile
from Arch Rock, and measures will be
taken to keep all craft safe. A party will
go out with the contractor to get a view
cf the final operations. Crowds will un
doubtedly gather vpon the north. shore
of the bay to witness the spectacle that
will be afforded. . .
To say that Castellano held his own
even measured against Salassa is to say
much, but barring the defections of his
voice Castellano's Otello did not suffer by
comparison with his great confrere's Iago.
It was a spirited, dignified and highly In
tellectual performance, and the role suits
Castellano vocally better than anything
else he has been heard in here, with its
demand for a high-pitched, brilliant vocal
organ. The ensemble work between the
two artists was a model of certainty, and
nothing so good in its way has been heard
here for many moons. It is also pleasing
to record that the audience, a delightfully
"Otello" grows on one with very hear
ing. It is the most modern of modern.
Italian music; greater, incomparably,
than the Puccini, Leoncavallo or Mas
cagnl school from every standpoint. It Is
eminently melodious, yet never descends
to the tricky tunefulness of some of Verdi's
earlier operas, their cheap and violent
effects. It Is dose akin in spirit to Wag
ner in its breadth of design, clearness of
construction and masterly handling of
material, and is a musical marvel as the
work of a man already past the allotted
three score years and ten at its writing.
As to the performance, it is "place aux
hommes" with "Otello" as given at th<?
Tivoli this week. Salassa excels himself
as Iago. He Is In admirable voice, ana
with his vibrant and eminently sympa
thetic song, marvelous facial expression
and graceful genius of gesture gives a su
perbly satisfactory interpretation of
Shakespeare's supple villain. With the
iest of the audience I can but say
"Bravo! Bravissima!" Salassa!
THE Tivoli management Is to be
congratulated on the very clever
fashion in which it has succeeded
in so far arranging its programme
as to insure increasing interest
with each performance. This is the third
week of the season, "Otello," given last
night, the sixth opera, and unquestionably
the best programme yet performed.
It is expected that Sheriff Veale will al
low Winters to remain in the City Prison
at the Hall of Justice until the balance
of the loot Is recovered from the waters
of the bay. He will then be taken to Mar
tinez and prosecuted. ;
"I won't tell you a thing about it," he
said, "and any statements that I have
talked about the matter are lies. But you
can bet that I won't go to prison and I
won't have to work for a long time.- I
know what I am talking about."
Winters is willing to talk about the
robbery and poses as a great criminal,
but he will not tell just how much he was
offered by ex-Chief of Police Lees to dis
close where he had placed his booty.
Winters dented yesterday that he had
told any one that he had been promised
$5000 and a short sentence in order to con
fess as to where the gold was hidden.
Sheriff Veale of Contra Costa. County
and Constable Moiles of Crockett 'were in
the city yesterday, but they did not visit
the Hall of Justice. Chief of Detectives
Seymour had sent word to the Sheriff that
he desired him to come after the robber,
but Veale did not put in an appearance
at the City Prison. Winters was told that
he wculd be taken to Contra Costa County
yesterday and he became very excited.
His conduct leads to the belief that he is
feigning insanity In order to escape his
well-deserved punishment.
The detectives were of opinion yester
day thnt the four missing bars of gold
had been placed by Winters in another
hiding place, and Detective 'Gibson came
over to the city from Crockett in order to
have a talk with the gold thief. Winters
assured the officer that he haul dumped
all the loot from the Selby vault at the
spot in the bay he had pointed out. De
tective Gibson wanted to take Winters
back to Crockett, but after talking with
the prisoner the detective hurried to
catch a train and return to the scene of
the robbery.
One of the bars recovered yesterday
bore a number not published in the orig
inal list of the stolen bullion. Un to yes
terday thÂ«s amount of gold 'bullion recov
ered figured ?2o3,000, and the Selbys state
that there are. still four bars to be fished
for, the value of the same being $65,000.
Detective Tom Gibson and Pinkerton
Agent Sayers spent yesterday morning in
diving at the spot where the gold was
dumped. They worked three hours. The
incoming tide put an end to their opera
tions.
Winters spent yesterday in the prison
at the Hall of Justice in this city, and
he kept up the game of "playing crazy"
whenever he was approached by news
paper representatives, j Â¦
It is believed by the detectives that
Winters did not place all the gold bars in
one spot. The sleuths are of the opinion
that Winters has hidden some of them
on land and that he hopes to secure them
inthe future, at the same time account
ing for the non-recovery of all the gold
from the bay by the excuse that spmeof
the bullion slipped down through the mud
into deep water.
Two more bricks, valued at $24,492, were
recovered yesterday.
Chief Bookkeeper Arthur Beam of the
Selby works stated last evening that a
revision of the books showed that Winters
had secured $320,000 worth of bullion.
A bar recovered yesterday from the
â¢waters of the bay near Vallejo Junction
bore a number not found in the printed
reward list.
John Winters, the robber, who looted
the vault of the Selby Smelting Works at
Vallejo Junction last Tuesday morning,
secured as his booty gold bullion to the
value of $320,000, or nearly $50,000 more
than the sum originally given. An error
was made in compiling the list of stolen
bars.
PEKING MAKES
PORT AT LAST
"It must not be supposed that the com
mission will not be able to accurately de
termine what the actual value of the land
is. An advisory committee has been ap
pointed, which includes as expert judges
of timber lands as there are in the State.
Among these are Captain Charles Nelson,
Robert Dollar, General N. P. Chtpman,
Hervey Lindley and W. W. Van Arsdale.
There is also expert knowledge of timber
lands on the part of two members of the
commission. It will not do for the lum
bermen to point out that a certain tree
will produce so many shakes, etc., and so
estimate its value. There are questions
concerning what it will cost to get it to
market and what the net profit will be.
"It is true that the commission, as The
Call has stated, was invited to go into the
Big Basin whiie the Sempervirens Club
was there as the guest of the club. It
might as well be understood that the
Commissioners do not wish to go there
under such conditions. We do not wish
to be under any obligations to any one.
Moreover, we will not deal with any one
except the owners of the land, and not
then until we have from them a cleancut
proposition, accompanied , by the neces
sary maps and descriptions. The Semper
virens Club has the land to the extent of
about 2500 acres under a bond. This bond
will expire September 1.
Commission's Position Stated.
One of the Commissioners said to a re
porter for The Call yesterday: "There is
no doubt, as The Call has set forth, that
the very lands that are held for the State
under the supposition that we will buy
them at $100 per acre have been sold in
the past eighteen months for very much
less. money. Indeed, we have found that
tracts in the very cream â¢ of the forest,
belonging, to Timothy Hopkins, have in
that period changed hands for $40 and $50
per acre. I personally might be willing to
give the Big Basin Lumber- Company an
advance on the actual value of the lands
for timber, considering that they are eli
gible for a park. Perhaps I- would even
allow them an advance of 25 per cent- in
some instances, but a profit Â¦ of 110 per
cent is altogether out of reason and is ex
tortionate.
the Big Basin country at $100 per
acre have been routed. The Call told first
of this plan, ani has since been vigorously
abused for that act by those who stood to
win if the deal could be carried through.
This journal desired to have , the State
pay only a fair price for the lands in
question. Its fearless presentation of facts
has borne good fruit. The Redwood Park
Commission will not submit to imposi
tion, nor will it take up any proposition'
without due and careful consideration.- A
park will be acquired. If the lumbermen
insist upon an extortionate price the right
of eminent domain will be used - in con
demnation proceedings,- and nothing but
the actual value will' be given in return
for the forest reservation which is sought
by the State. Good citizens will hail this
victory of The Call for the > public from
one end of the State to the other. -

planning for. the past year or more
! to make a handsome profit out of
the State in the sale of lands in

Possible Hotel Scheme.
"The Commissioners were Informed
some time ago that two tracts would be
offered to the State. One of these.em
braced 700 acres and the other 1200 acres.
Between the two tracts was a. distance of
about two miles. Of course, a hotel could
be placed by private Individuals In that
intervening space, and the work of the
Commissioners in improving the park
would be -largely in the nature of making
grounds for a hotel if we took up with
any such idea. At our first meeting we
therefore voted that all the lands must be
contiguous. Since then a tract has been
suggested which includes the â¢ 1200-acre
tract but not the 700-acre tract, the latter
including the choicest timber. Around
the 1200-acre tract are other lands which
the commission will be offered to make
up a tract of i500 acres, so I am credibly
Informed. But those lands are not as
good as those which Timothy Hopkins
sold a few months ago for $40 and $50 per
acre. In the tract of 2500 acres, so made
up, is a jog which includes about 300
acres. The explanation given by Mr.
Middleton concerning the exclusion of
this jog from the tract is that there is
litigation concerning the ownership. That
explanation is not good, for the State
can condemn the land just the same and
settle with the owners when the courts
determine where the title Is vested.
Cannot Deal With, dub.
"Possibly that ' jog might be used by
private individuals for a hotel site if the
State bought the tract that surrounded it
on three sides. We will not deal with the
Sempervirens Club. I think that the club
people are honest lovers of the forest and
are all right, but possibly they are being
used. When a- club appears to be urging
the establishment of a park that gives
the enterprise a look of public spirit.- If
the Same, thing was done by private own
ers the motive of selfishness would be
more easily j inferred. Every one under
stands that when there is State money
to expend there is an expectation of get
ting a big price. There will be no realiza
tion of anything of the sort in this in
stance. Every tract of forty acres will
have the timber upon it estimated by ex
perts. The real value will not be difficult
to reach."
This authoritative statement of one of
the Commissioners in behalf: of the board
will awaken much interest. In the Big
Basin great preparations were being: made
for the coming of the Governor and the
commission. . Buildings were erected on
the east fork of Waddell Creek, and also
tents, the entire outfit being called "the
Governor's camp." This, it is reported,
with the other expenses Involved In put
ting up tanks to water the road leading
into the Big Basin, the clearing up of
good trails from the Sempervirens' camp
to the Governor's camp, etc., have cost
H. L. Middleton and the other men asso
ciated with him in the Big Basin Lumber
Company about $3000. The company could
well afford this outlay if the devised plan
could be carried through. The names of
the incorporators of the Big Basin Lum
ber Company have been already published
in connection with the original expose in
The Call. The largest individual owner
of timber lands in the Big Basin is Tim
othy Hopkins. He is also largely Inter
ested in the Big Basin Lumber Company.
